## ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS

It is impossible to acknowledge all those who contributed to the conception of this book, but my special thanks must go to my teachers at Reading University, many of whom it has since been my good fortune to count as colleagues. I am grateful to Professor Anthony Betts, whose guidance of a general kind has been of lasting value, Frank Ormrod, who introduced me to the practical side of lithography, and H. V. Redman, for his support and encouragement at a vital stage. I am particularly indebted to Professor L. D. Ettlinger, whose teaching of Renaissance art history inspired me to take an interest in the work of the past and prompted me to undertake a modest piece of research of my own. If this book has any merit at all it is largely due to his example.

I am grateful to the staff of many libraries and print rooms and particularly to Mr. Turner Berry and Mr. James Mosley of the St. Bride Printing Library, Mr. Harry Carter and Mrs. Thrussell (who cared for the John Johnson Collection while it was at the University Press, Oxford), and successive Librarians and the staff of my own university library. All have been most helpful and have offered generous facilities over a number of years. I should also like to thank Dr. B. Degenhart of the Staatliche Graphische Sammlung and Dr. R A. Winkler for the kindness they showed me while I was working in Munich.

For permission to reproduce items in their collections acknowledgements are due to the Librarians or Curators of the following: Bibliothèque Nationale, Cabinet des Estampes, 71-3; Bodleian Library, John Johnson Collection, 92, 95-7, 99–101, 103-4, 107-12; British Museum, 1, 65–7, 74–5, 89, 115-16; Cambridge University Library, 93-4, 98, 105, 122–3, 137–46, 149-54, 156–7; Ministry of Defence Library, 2, 6–10; Reading University Library, 34-5, 38, 40, 42–9, 55-7, 78–9, 117–18; St. Bride Printing Library, 3-5, 12–33, 37, 39, 41, 50–4, 58–64, 69–70, 76–7, 84-5, 106, 158; Victoria and Albert Museum, 11, 102, 119, 136, 155.

Mr. Harry Carter, Mr. S. A. Kitzinger, Mr. H. V. Redman, and Dr. Berthold Wolpe have read the book either in typescript or at proof stage and have all made valuable suggestions. Mrs. Caroline Lessiter, Mr. O. C. Giles, and Dr. A. Menhennet have helped with translation problems.

My thanks are also due to Mr. I. McLean and the staff of the Photographic Department of Reading University Library for their patience and skill in meeting my requirements for the illustrations, and to the Publisher and Printer for the care they have taken in the preparation and presentation of this volume.

Finally, I must thank my wife for her understanding and tolerance, and for nursing me through the final stages of the work.

# INTRODUCTION

I HIS BOOK IS NOT INTENDED TO BE A COMPREHENSIVE HISTORY OF lithography. It is a study of the various techniques used by artists in England and France for making drawings on stone, and of the use inade of these methods in topographical and landscape prints. My main concern has been to describe what seem to have been the accepted techniques of the professional lithographic draughtsman and the way these developed with improvements to the process and with the changing needs of artists. I have not discussed the work of the great painters who occasionally turned to lithography, nor have I described the many commercial aspects of the process; the work of the painter-lithographer has been the central interest of most writers on lithography, while a study of the commercial development of the process would have opened up an entirely different field which, I hope, may one day be the subject of a companion volume.

The period covered by this book is roughly the first fifty years of lithography, but I have concentrated on the decades after 1818 when the process became widely known through the publication of treatises by the inventor and other lithographic printers. At the tine of writing, however, no serious introduction to the early history of lithography had appeared in English since J. and E. R. Pennell's Lithography and lithographers, which was originally published in 1898. In Part I of the book, therefore, I have discussed the invention of lithography and have given a short account of its development up to the year 1818 in Germany, England, and France, in order to provide some kind of background for the chapters on the early literature. I have included no illustrations for this section. Many of the more important items referred to in the text have been reproduced elsewhere and, tempting though it was to include a variety of plates, it seemed to me more valuable to concentrate on illustrating the main theme of the book.

Part II is concerned with the development of the actual techniques of drawing on stone as they were described in contemporary literature, from the patent specifications and early accounts in journals to technical treatises devoted to the subject. The first writers of notices about lithography in the journals were mainly concerned with making it known and describing briefly how it worked; for the most part, however, they seem to have known very little about the process, and their accounts are mainly ofinterest as records of the progress lithography was making in becoming recognized as a reliable graphic and printing process. Only later, with the publication of books entirely devoted to the subject, did descriptions become more informative. The first such manual appeared in Germany in 181o, but the turning point was really the publication of the inventor's own treatise in 1818, and thereafter technical handbooks on lithography were published regularly. Most of them were written by lithographic printers and were directed at either the prospective lithographic printer or draughtsman, or both. Apart from Senefelder's, the most influential of these manuals were first published in France and England. Lithography developed similarly in these two countries, and in terms of the literature on the subject it is almost impossible to treat them separately since the techniques described in the one country were almost immediately adopted by artists and writers in the other. Articles on lithography continued to appear in journals during this later period, and these sometimes contained accounts of new methods which were not described elsewhere, but it is in the technical manuals that we can best trace the growth of the various techniques used by artists in the first half of the nineteenth century for making drawings on stone.

In Part III of the book I have traced the development of one branch of lithography in England and France, the making of topographical prints. I have discussed the ways in which the techniques described in the treatises were actually applied and have tried to relate them to the requirements of contemporary painters and draughtsmen. My main reason for selecting this particular branch of pictorial lithography was that it was extremely popular and flourished equally strongly in both France and England throughout the period under consideration.

The artists who turned to landscape and topography tended to work along the lines of the techniques described in the manuals on lithography. This book is, therefore, largely concerned with the work of the craftsmanlithographer. It is not suggested that the methods described here applied to all artists who drew on stone in this period. We would not expect the great

## Introduction

painters of the period to approach lithography in the same spirit as the professional landscape lithographer. The evidence of the prints of Goya and Delacroix are sufficient testimony to this, even without the descriptions we have of their methods of drawing on stone; but neither of these artists could entirely escape from the recognized conventions of the medium, and the extent to which they departed from them can only be appreciated when we know what these conventions were.

# THE FIRST TWENTY YEARS OF LITHOGRAPHY 1798-1818

# THE INVENTION AND SPREAD OF LITHOGRAPHY

VV HEN LITHOGRAPHY WAS DISCOVERED BY SENEFELDER TOWARDS the end of the eighteenth century no really new method of making prints had been evolved for over three hundred years. The older methods of woodcutting on the one hand and engraving and etching on the other were firmly established printing crafts—woodcutting being valued primarily because of its simplicity and economy, and etching and engraving for their greater precision and for the wider range of expression they allowed the artist. By the end of the eighteenth century, however, these original methods had been found inadequate. The requirements of painters had caused these processes to increase their range of effects so that in their turn the related methods of mezzotint, stipple engraving, aquatint, soft-ground etching, and engraving on box-wood were developed to cater for the new needs. But these were all still relatively slow, either in the production of the original plate or in the printing from it, and the growing demand for more and cheaper images encouraged the search for new and less laborious processes. The period around the turn of the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries was, therefore, one of considerable experimentation in the graphic arts and numerous ingenious processes were devised, either with a view to improving on existing methods or in the hope of finding cheaper ones. Lithography was the result of just one of these experiments and, at least at its outset, must fall into the second of these two categories.

The term lithography is a little misleading, for writing on stone, or even printing from it, is not what constitutes the invention. In retrospect it is looked upon as the first and principal method of planographic printing in which, in contrast to the relief and intaglio processes, the areas which print and those which do not are to all intents and purposes on the same level. The process rests on very simple chemical principles: the antipathy of grease and water, and the attraction of these two substances to their like and to a common porous ground. It is capable of considerable complication, and some of these developments will be considered in the following chapters, but it can perhaps be best understood in its very simplest form. If a cross is drawn with a candle or any other greasy substance on a slab of porous material, such as stone, it will adhere to it and be partially absorbed. If the whole surface is then moistened, the water will be attracted by the stone but repelled by the greasy cross. For the same reasons any subsequent application of greasy printing ink will be repelled by the damped surface of the stone except in those parts drawn with the cross. In this part alone, where there is no water, grease will be attracted by grease. It remains therefore to lay a piece of paper or similar material on to the surface of the stone and to apply pressure to its reverse side. The resulting print we should call a lithograph.

This was not the term originally used by either the inventor or by the earliest lithographers. Senefelder does not appear to have used it until 18o8 and even on the title-page of his treatise of 1818² it creeps in only as though to cover all contingencies. The French seem to have coined the word first and the term ‘imprimerie lithographique' is found on an undated piece of music printing which was registered as early as 18o3.³ It appears again in the following year on a prospectus for André's press in Paris dated ‘1er Frimaire an 13',4 and the term ‘l’art lithographique'figures in literature at least by July 1807.⁵ From France the term seems to have spread to Germany where it was used in 18o5 by Mitterer for the title of a collection of lithographs,⁶ and later to England where it was fairly generally adopted after 1813. But everywhere the process went under à variety of names during the first twenty years of its existence. In Germany it was originally called ‘Steindruck', Steindruckerey', or ‘chemische Druckerey' and even as late as 1814 these were the terms still in general use there.2 In England it was usually referred to as ‘polyautography', but sometimes as just ‘engraving on stone' or printing from stone'. In France either ‘imprimerie chimique' or ‘polyautographie' were the most common early terms until the word lithographie' came into something like general use there around 18ro. If Senefelder himself had coined a suitable term for his invention he might well have found no rival claimants for priority, for much of this variety in terminology reflected a confusion as to what really constituted the invention.

Alois Senefelder was born in Prague in 1771, the son of an actor from the Theatre Royal in Munich. He too wanted to become an actor, but at the insistence of his father was sent to study jurisprudence at the University of Ingolstadt. It was while still a student there in 1789 that Senefelder wrote a little comedy, Die Mädchenkenner, which was to provide him with an introduction to printing when he had it published in Munich in 1792. On the death of his father in 1791 he was forced to give up his studies, only too pleased it would seem to concentrate on his chosen professions of acting and writing. After two years as an unsuccessful actor he set out to try his fortune as an author, but again he came up against difficulties for his first play scarcely covered his printing costs. It was in an attempt to publish his own plays rather more cheaply that Senefelder began to learn about printing.³ First he studied letterpress methods, but lacking sufficient capital he was forced to experiment on his own account and, like so many others of his generation, tried various kinds of stereotyping. These, too, cost more than his limited means would allow, and so he turned to etching by the normal method on either copper or tin. While practising the rather awkward business of writing backwards for this process Senefelder was attracted by a piece of Kelheim limestone which he had been using as an inking slab. In the first instance it occurred to him that this would be much more economical than the copper for these practice attempts, and only later, when he realized that the stone could be obtained of sufficient thickness, that this too could be used as a printing surface. He was aware that this was not a new process, but he made two distinct improvements which he thought were of considerable importance. The first of these was the discovery of a method of giving the stone a higher polish, and the second the manufacture of an ink which could easily be wiped away from the surface once the image had been charged. It was in fact this ink, composed of wax, soap, and lampblack, which paved the way for the later discovery of lithography. But this did not come immediately, for there was yet another stage that the inventor had to go through before arriving at planographic printing; he had already tried an intaglio method, he was now to experiment with relief printing.

Senefelder explains in the most quoted passage from his treatise how he had just polished one of his stone plates ready to coat it with etching ground in preparation for his exercises in writing backwards when his mother came in with the now celebrated laundry list which, for want of suitable writing materials, he was forced to copy out on the stone with his specially prepared ink. Some time afterwards he was just about to obliterate this writing when it occurred to him that he might be able to put the image in relief by etching it with dilute nitric acid. He did so for five minutes, and found that the writing had been left in relief about one-twentieth of an inch. He made a number of further trials, inking the stone with a board covered with cloth, and produced results which he found greatly encouraging. Thus, writes Senefelder rather strangely, the new art was invented'.' What he meant by this is not quite clear, but if he meant that this was lithography then he cannot reasonably claim to have been its inventor, because relief printing from stone had certainly been practised before. It is true that he had constructed a new lever press, that he had discovered an ingenious method of inking with a flat board which enabled him to print from an image lower in relief than if he had used the normal inking balls, and that he had developed a new ink which acted as an acid resist; but none of these constituted the invention of a new process. This method of printing was certainly not lithography as we know it today for it depended principally on a difference of relief; moreover, in his account of these experiments, Senefelder makes no mention of either damping the stone or of applying gum arabic.'

A similar process had in fact been practised by Dean Schmidt only a few years before and in Munich itself. Simon Schmidt of Miesbach had, according to his own testimony,² printed from relief-etched stone while he was professor of logic and natural history at the Military Academy in Munich (1787–8). He made a number of drawings for German school-books, including one on the human body and another on poisonous plants. There was nothing new therefore in printing from stone, and though Senefelder claimed no knowledge of Schmidt's process at the time of his own experiments he certainly knew about it when he came to write his treatise.³ By this time Schmidt was being acclaimed in some quarters as the inventor of lithography; and if this is taken to mean printing from stones then it must be admitted that Schmidt was working well before Senefelder began his own relief etching in July 1796. But in any case Schmidt never claimed the process as his own and freely admitted that he had been prompted to make experiments on seeing some tombstones in Munich which he thought had been produced by this method, and that he had even followed closely the instructions laid down in an old Nuremberg handbook.⁴ A similar idea of relief etching on copper seemed to have occurred quite independently to William Blake in England at much the same time, and others followed along similar lines in France and England. Relief etching, whether on stone or metal, had many of the advantages of stereotyping without the added inconvenience and expense of having to set up type. Senefelder was in fact neither the first nor the last to experiment on stone along these lines, but merely provides a link in a chain which was to lead from Schmidt to Duplat,' Girardet,² Tissier,3 and many others in the nineteenth century. At the time, however, relief etching was, so far as we are aware, new to Senefelder. But what is so surprising is that as late as 1818, long after the discovery of lithography proper, Senefelder insisted on referring to this relief etching on stone as his new invention.4 His treatise is full of such ambiguities, as though he himself was not quite sure what his real contribution was. But in this particular case, by maintaining that this relief method—a process in which we know Schmidt to have had priority—was his invention, Senefelder was providing later historians with a convenient tool with which to wrest from him the real invention of lithography.

For about two years, from 1796 to 1798, Senefelder practised his relief etching on stone. At the outset, in order to raise capital, he travelled to

Ingolstadt to enlist as a private in the artillery as a substitute for a friend. His application was rejected as he was not a Bavarian by birth, but his journey to Ingolstadt was not entirely in vain for he noticed a piece of badly printed music in a shop there, and it was this that suggested to him that his process could be applied to the printing of music. On his return to Munich he decided to approach a publisher, and would have done so had he not learned that an old musician friend of his, the composer Gleissner, was about to publish some pieces of sacred music. He called on Gleissner, showed him specimens of his work, and arranged to print an edition of some of his songs. One hundred and twenty copies of Gleissner's 12 Neue Lieder für's Klavier' (1796) were printed on a copper-plate press, the whole undertaking being completed with the help of one man in the space of a fortnight, and costing only 3o florins. Encouraged by this success Senefelder presented a copy to the Elector Karl Theodor and received a gift of 1oo florins. Another work for Gleissner followed, ‘Duetten für zwei Flöten', and everything appeared to be most promising until Senefelder decided to replace his existing rolling-press with an improved version. It was then that he met difficulties and was forced to experiment with a variety of presses, even resorting to rubbing by hand with a piece of polished wood. Eventually he got the backing of Falter, a music seller in Munich, and constructed a large cylinder press which enabled him to print much better. As soon as this press was completed he began to copy Mozart's Die Zauberflöte on to stone and started printing it with the help of Gleissner. All went well so long as Gleissner was there, but when he fell dangerously ill and was no longer available Senefelder had to train two soldiers to do the printing. It was when they took over that Falter decided to revert to copper-engraving.

At this stage Senefelder must surely have heard something of Schmidt's experiments,¹ for he came into contact with Steiner who, in the position of Direktor des Schulfonds-Bücherlags', had sponsored and printed several of the latter's publications. Steiner approached Senefelder with a project for printing some church music. The original intention was to insert the lines of music between the letterpress and print them both together, but this proved too difficult and eventually the two opcrations were separated.²

With the experience of Schmidt's illustrations behind him, Steiner saw more possibilities for Senefelder's relief etching than just the printing of music, and even Senefelder credits him with being one of the first to display a liberal and impartial spirit in encouraging his process. Senefelder had himself already drawn a small vignette on stone for a piece of music entitled 'Der Brand in Neuötting' in August 1797, and it was this which prompted Steiner to commission him to make some illustrations for a Catechism. According to Senefelder the resulting drawings were very ordinary, but at least Steiner had seen that there were greater possibilities in the process than the reproduction of writing and music. Senefelder was no great draughtsman and, consequently, when Steiner suggested that he should produce rather more ambitious religious scenes as a cheap alternative to engraving, he thought the time had come to teach others how to draw on stone. A number of young artists were instructed, but none of them showed sufficient patience to become skilled and they soon lost interest. So too it would seem did Senefelder, for he had just become interested in the experiments which were to lead him to the invention of planographic printing and lithography proper.

At the time he was engaged in writing a prayer book on stone, and this was causing him considerable trouble because all the letters had to be drawn in reverse. His normal method was to offset a whole page of text which had been written on paper in lead pencil, and then work over this image with his prepared ink; but the pencil marks were easily rubbed off the stone, and so he experimented with a variety of more permanent compositions. These trials led him to realize that it would be possible to compose an ink which would be sufficient for both purposes; that is, which could be transferred from the paper but would need no reworking on the stone. All together he claims to have made several thousand experiments before discovering a suitable recipe for the ink and a method of preparing the paper with gum which would allow this ink to be removed in its entirety. But having succeeded in this he was brought to the verge of the discovery of lithography, for he realized that the whole process of transferring ‘rested principally on the greater or less affinity of one ingredient to another'.' He noticed, for instance, that a viscous liquid like a solution of gum prevented the ink from attaching itself to the stone, and that if he drew with soap on a newlypolished stone and moistened its surface with gum-water an application of oil colour adhered only to those places covered with the soap. The main difficulty he found at this stage was that the stone was too highly polished to receive the ink, and that the image tended to spread. He therefore washed the stone with soap-water or linseed oil before beginning to write, and then, when the work was finished, cleaned the stone with a solution of nitric acid to prevent the surface taking ink during the printing. This is how Senefelder summarizes his invention:

My whole process was, therefore, as follows:—to wash the polished stone with soap-water, to dry it well, to write or draw upon it with the composition ink of soap and wax, then to etch it with aqua-fortis; and, lastly, to prepare it for printing with an infusion of gum-water. I had hoped to have been able to dispense with the gumwater, but was soon convinced that it really enters into chemical affinity with the stone, and stops its pores still more effectually against the fat, and opens them to the water. In less than three days after my first idea, I produced as perfect and clear impressions, as any that have since been obtained.

In this context Senefelder gives no date for his invention, but ten pages earlier he writes of ‘the chemical printing, which I discovered in 1798'² and on one of the plates of the German edition of his treatise there is a reference to ‘Der Steindruck erfunden zu München von Aloys Senefelder 1796. Durch ihn zur chenischen Druckerey erhoben 1798.' In the text Senefelder records this with less excitement than he had shown for his earlier ‘invention' of relief etching on stone, and for the historian the account certainly lacks the drama of the laundry-list episode; but this was, nevertheless, the invention of lithography, and there can be little justification for the centenary celebrations which were held in various parts of the world in 1895 and 1896.1

The very first work Senefelder published by means of his new process was not produced by working in a direct manner with his specially prepared ink. Very soon after this discovery he perfected the chalk and engraved manners, and it was by using the second of these that he produced the title-page for Gleissner's Eine Symphonie von vier obligaten Stimmen'. With these researches behind him Senefelder at last seemed to be in a position to exploit his process. By 1799 he was receiving work from Steiner and others to the value of 10-12 florins a day, and he employed his brothers Theobald and Georg as well as two apprentices. On 3 September of the same year he was granted an exclusive privilege for the whole of Bavaria to last for a period of fifteen years,² and he now felt free to show his process to anyone who was interested in it.

One such person was Johann Anton André, a music publisher from Offenbach, who was so impressed by the speed³ and economy of the process that he offered Senefelder 2,ooo florins for a description of the process and for the right to exploit it. Senefelder accepted the offer and on 28 September 1799, within a month of his being granted the exclusive privilege, he and Gleissner signed a contract with André.4 The contract provided that they should explain the whole of the process in every detail and that Senefelder should travel to Offenbach to search for suitable lithographic stone there and, with the help of André's brother Friedrich, have all the necessary machinery and equipment made for the setting up of a lithographic printing works.

After an interval of three months Senefelder travelled to Offenbach and within a fortnight pulled his first proof there. André must have been most impressed by what he had seen of the process, for he suggested that Senefelder should leave Munich and enter into partnership with him. His idea was to establish presses at Vienna, London, Paris, and Berlin, and to take out privileges or patents in each of these capitals. Offenbach was to be the centre of the organization, and André intended to remain there himself, while Senefelder undertook to be in charge of the press at Vienna. With this in mind Senefelder returned to Munich and arranged to hand over his own press there to his brothers, reserving for himself a quarter of the profits. Back in Offenbach he found the new establishment in good order with five of André's old presses replaced by lithographic ones; everything appeared to be going well and soon afterwards André took the opportunity of a business trip to London to inquire about the taking out of patents there. In England they had hoped to apply lithography to the printing of cottons, especially as Senefelder had in mind a method of printing with stone cylinders. André had even intended taking out a patent for this idea, but he found that machines for printing cottons with cylindrical metal plates were already in use in England,² and was forced to abandon the project. André's plans for lithography itself were not to be interrupted though, and on his return to Germany he persuaded Senefelder to go to London with Friedrich André to lodge a description of the process and take out a patent for it. From late in 180o to the summer of 1801 Senefelder was in London, and on 20 June I8oi a patent was taken out in his name.³

In London Senefelder was met by Philipp André, another brother who was living there at the time, and Senefelder records rather bitterly that it was he who looked after his affairs during his stay. Senefelder left England before 10 August 18or, for on that date he signed a further document in Offenbach relinquishing all the privileges granted in the above-mentioned patent to Johann André for the sum of 3,ooo florins.⁴

On his return to Germany Senefelder found that his mother had gone to Vienna to obtain an exclusive privilege for his two brothers, and that as a counter-measure André had sent Gleissner's wife to act on behalf of their own partnership. Senefelder claims that he had been kept in the dark about this while he was in London, and that when André refused to allow him to go to Vienna to sort the affair out he tore up the contract of partnership which had been signed only the previous day. The unhappy position in which he had found himself, of being a party to both sides of the squabble, was thus made a lot easier, and having made peace with his brothers he decided to take them with him to Vienna.

Thus ended a most promising liaison between the ingenious but unbusinesslike inventor² and the efficient and imaginative businessman André which, had it lasted, would almost certainly have seen lithography firmly established in the capitals of Europe within a few years. As it was, the rewards were divided, and neither party was able to take full advantage of his share. Senefelder had gained capital to the extent of 5,ooo florins, but had forfeited his right to print in England and had given André the necessary knowledge and equipment to do so in Bavaria; André on the other hand had lost the services of a skilled craftsman and technician who was still working to perfect and extend his invention and was, in all probability, the only man who really understood how it worked.

Senefelder left for Vienna in August 18o1³ and stayed there for five years. Gleissner's wife had already found him a likely patron in the form of von Hartl, who had seen the possibilities of lithography for calico printing, and as soon as he arrived Senefelder constructed a small press and pulled some proofs as a demonstration of the process. These so impressed von Hartl that he offered him a partnership with sufficient capital to equip himself for printing. The principal intention was to apply lithography to cotton printing, but until this could be begun they were to set up a press for printing music and works of art. Their first concern was to get an exclusive privilege to print by the new process, and as soon as possible Senefelder gave a demonstration before a commission set up by the Government of Lower Austria, and even made several trials on a little hand press before the Emperor himself. Meanwhile, stones were procured from Solenhofen and paper from

Augsburg; his old associate Gleissner was brought from Munich to help, and for nearly a year they printed virtually nothing but Gleissner's musical compositions. Eventually, on 13 January 1803, an exclusive privilege for ten years was obtained for Lower Austria and the way seemed open to success. The biggest difficulty was the sale of the works they produced, for large stocks of Gleissner's music remained on their hands, so instead of relying on agents they decided to sell their own products. Accordingly, a business company was formed between Senefelder, Steiner (the secretary of von Hartl), and a businessman called Grasnitzky. For a while Senefelder's hopes were rejuvenated, but they were soon dashed when he was told that no profits would come his way until he had paid back the capital of 2o,ooo florins advanced by von Hartl. In desperation, and without the financial means to defend himself, he sold out his share of the establishment to Steiner, and with it surrendered his Austrian privilege as well.

Senefelder's only hope now was the calico printing that had interested von Hartl. In fact, during the whole of his stay in Vienna he had needed to devote very little of his time to the routine running of the lithographic press for, in addition to Gleissner, he had brought one of his first apprentices from Munich, and also employed two local music writers. He had been mainly concerned himself with experiments in printing cottons by means of etched metal cylinders, and with the help of an English engineer had even constructed such a press at the cotton centre of Pottendorf. Unfortunately, just as he was in a position to sell his invention, supplies of English spun cotton were cut off because of the Napoleonic Wars and the demand on the spinning factory at Pottendorf was so great that they had to put aside any new project. Eventually, Senefelder managed to sell a model of his cylinder press to a calico printer in Vienna, and soon afterwards was commissioned by another firm to erect one at St. Pölten. He was on the point of leaving for St. Pölten to fulfil this obligation when there came a far more attractive offer from Abbot Vogler and Christoph von Aretin to set up, at their expense, a lithographic press in Munich with his old friend Gleissner. After arranging to delay the construction of his press at St. Pölten, Senefelder left with Gleissner for Munich in October 18o6.

Almost immediately the partnership was dissolved because Vogler refused to pay his share of the capital in cash; but Aretin, Gleissner, and Senefelder continued together for a period of about four years, producing a variety of jobbing printing, music, and maps, as well as what Senefelder refers to as‘works of art'. In this category must be included the most important production of the press, and the first of its kind, the facsimile edition of Dürer's marginal drawings for the Emperor Maximilian's Prayer Book, published in 18o8. There were five presses at this Munich establishment, but it was not an economic success as some of the work which Senefelder printed for the government had to be produced in enormous editions, and often in a great hurry, so that rather more presses and printers had to be kept than were really needed. All the same, this appears to have been the most settled period of Senefelder's career. Scholars, statesmen, and royalty visited his press, and it was in this period that he thought about writing a treatise on lithography and began printing the first collection of specimens for his Musterbuch über alle lithographischen Kunstmanieren in 1808.

In October of the following year Senefelder received his first real recompense from lithography. Despite his privilege a number of other presses had already been established in Munich, including one belonging to the Königliche unmittelbare Steuer-Kataster-Kommission'. The aim of this establishment was to survey and map the whole of the country, and between thirty and forty draughtsmen were employed to draw on stone the numerous plans that were needed. It appears that there was some dissatisfaction over the way the lithographic printing was being done, and Senefelder was invited to supervise this side of the establishment with an annual salary of 1,50o florins. Moreover, he was still allowed to continue his private press with Aretin. At last everything seemed to be going Senefelder's way; but just as his own lithographic press was beginning to flourish Aretin was appointed to a post in Neuberg and they were forced to sell the business.

This left Senefelder with more free time to devote to writing his treatise and to experiment with the many ideas he had for the improvement of lithography. Amongst these were the perfection of the transfer method, and particularly the transferring of leaves of old books and prints; the discovery of a method of colour printing; the construction of a printing press in which the damping, cleaning, and inking of the stone could be done mechanically; and, in Senefelder's view probably the most important of all, the application of lithography to metal and composition plates. These experiments must have been largely responsible for holding up work on the treatise; but eventually Senefelder was goaded into publication by allegations in the press that he was not the real inventor of lithography, and his book was at last published in Munich and Vienna in 1818. A year later translations appeared in London and Paris, and in connection with the French edition Senefelder made the first of a number of trips to Paris with his pupil Knecht, and there set up a press of his own to print the plates.' While in Paris he took out a patent for his composition stone in 1819,² perfected his portable press, and experimented with a printing machine in which both the inking and damping were done mechanically. He finally returned to Munich in 1824 having resigned his share of the press to Knecht for an annual payment of 6oo francs.

In Bavaria, with his treatise published and with an assured income from two sources, Senefelder continued to experiment in the hope of perfecting his process. He even intended publishing another work on lithography to include descriptions of his latest improvements, and in a letter to Knecht about this project Senefelder writes:

J'enrichirai cette partie de mon ouvrage d’une foule d’inventions nouvelles qui sont toutes du domaine de l’impression, par exemple, une composition excellente de pierres artificielles, l’impression mosaïque (invention inestimable), une nouvelle méthode de stéréotypie, une presse mécanique accélérée dont j’ai déjà obtenu des résultats, et sur laquelle l'encrage se fait mécaniquement . . . J'espère, par ces découvertes, avoir porté la lithographie au plus haut degré de perfection.³

But like so many of Senefelder's projects this too remained unfulfilled at his death in 1834. In any case, the things that occupied him in the twenties were still substantially the same as those which had done so in the previous decade; and it could hardly have been otherwise for, with the exception of the application of photography and the discovery of the principle of offset printing, Senefelder had already anticipated almost every other major development of his process.

![](images/f6e31f3b31f2eb4cae595b1c90e5e3b3cf8b3ffa0304c36118ebad4d0c1fb720.jpg)

# SUBSEQUENT DEVELOPMENT IN GERMANY UP TO 1818

ENEFELDER'S PRIVILEGE OF I799, WHICH GAVE HIM, AND LATER André, the exclusive right to use his process in Bavaria, proved to be virtually ineffectual. It is true that he managed to stop his brothers when they first tried to set up a press in Munich, and that later he prevented Strohofer from doing the same thing, but these appear to have been two of the rare occasions on which Senefelder was able to exercise his rights. And when Aretin complained to the proper authorities in Munich about the other lithographic presses that had been established there he was virtually told that since the art was no longer a secret they could expect nothing else, as though, adds Senefelder, keeping it a secret had been the condition of the privilege'.'

Presses infringing Senefelder's privilege were established in a number of Bavarian towns and many more were set up in Prussia, Austria, and the other German states. By 182o lithography had been introduced at one time or another into Augsburg, Berlin, Breslau, Durlach, Karlsruhe, Dresden, Hamburg, Heidelburg, Kassel, Leipzig, Mannheim, Offenbach, Prague, Regensburg, Straubing, and Stuttgart. But Munich still remained the centre for lithography at the beginning of the nineteenth century,² and Senefelder himself reveals that even as early as 18o9 there were six other presses working there, apart from a number established by amateurs for their own private use.' Naturally enough most of the earliest printers stemmed directly or indirectly from Senefelder's own press. In the first instance he trained his own brothers and they provided one of the principal means of disseminating the art in Germany. As early as 1799 Theobald and Georg Senefelder broke away and tried to found a press with the publisher Gombart in Augsburg, and Franz Anton Niedermayr learned the secret from the brothers and set up a press at Straubing in 180oo which he transferred to Regensburg in 1802. Grünewald, a former apprentice of Alois Senefelder, left to set up a press in Leipzig some time after 18o4 and one of Karl Senefelder's workmen, Strohofer, bought the secret from his master in 18o6 and began printing in Stuttgart in the spring of 1807.

The original lithographic press which Senefelder had established in Munich for his own use was taken over by his two brothers, Theobald and Georg. They continued to print there until 18o4 when the press passed to the ‘Feiertagsschule für Künstler und Techniker’ under the direction of Professor Hermann Joseph Mitterer.² A lithographic press had already been set up there by Niedermayr some time before this, and Mitterer had even made two drawings on stone as early as 18o1, but Theobald and Georg were called in to put lithography on a sounder basis and to instruct Mitterer in the technical side of the process for an annual sum of 7oo florins. Under Mitterer's guidance lithography made great progress, and he played a leading role in Munich as a popularizer of the art and as a sponsor of artists.³ Mitterer used the process mainly as an aid to teaching and found that it was ideally suited to the production of cheap versions of his own and other artists' drawings which could be used as models for his students to copy. Two oblong quarto works of this kind, a set of sixty-six crayon drawings of the human figure showing its construction on a basis of simple fornms and in relation to classical sculpture,4 and another set of plates of arabesques, geometrical shapes, decorative foliage and flowers,5 were published as early as 18o4. Though these works carry on from the tradition of engraved drawing-books, lithography was much quicker than either etching or engraving on copper, and it had a particular advantage in that pencil and chalk drawings could be imitated by using the crayon on stone. This was a branch of lithography that Mitterer did much to perfect; early historians erroneously credited him with being the inventor of the chalk manner, while even Senefelder himself admitted that it was a branch of lithography in which Mitterer achieved a degree of perfectionthat may properly be termed the ne plus ultra of the art'.² Mitterer's other main contribution to lithography was that in 18o5 he invented the cylinder or star-wheel press, which Senefelder considered to have been the only improvement to his invention which had not stemmed directly or indirectly from him.³

Some of the artists who contributed to Mitterer's educational works also produced prints for their own sake, and in 1805 Mitterer began publishing a collection of prints by Hauber, Klotz, Mayrhofer, Seidel, Wagenbauer, and Warnberger with the title Lithographische Kunstprodukte.4 This was a landmark in German lithography, for not only was the new name for the process used for the first time there on its wrappers, but it was the first important collection of artists' lithographs to be published in Germany. A set of small and rather immature landscape lithographs by E. J. Aurnhammer had been published as early as 1802,5 but no lithographic publication of the scale and quality of the Lithographische Kunstprodukte had yet appeared in Germany. Publication began in October 18o5 and continued in twenty-six monthly parts until December 1807, the entire work consisting of 156 lithographs. Each part contained six plates, page size approximately 18%× 12½ in., and consisted of two flower drawings, two landscapes, and two figure subjects or portraits. The six artists who contributed were some of the most prolific of the early Bavarian lithographers and the publication contains some of the most competent lithographs of the period. J. N. Mayrhofer, who specialized in flowers, and the landscape artist Max Wagenbauer were the most regular contributors, and between them account for over 10o of the plates. Mayrhofer's flowers were drawn in the idiom of eighteenthcentury flower plates, and his careful and unobtrusive chalk-work provided an ideal basis for hand-colouring.' Wagenbauer's picturesque landscapes were more fully drawn in chalk and do not rely on hand-colouring in the same way; their foregrounds in particular were handled with a consistently laid, but rather coarse, system of hatching and need no additional treatment. Similar techniques of hatching were employed by other artists in the figure and portrait plates of the collection, and those of Joseph Hauber are drawn with a particularly open and rigid system of lines which derives from the traditional syntax of the copper engraver. Though few of the plates in the Lithographische Kunstprodukte are of any real artistic significance, they are of a technical standard not achieved until about 1818 in France and 182o in England.

The other important lithographic establishment in Munich was the one Senefelder himself managed, trading under the style ‘A. Senefelder, Fr. Gleissner & Comp.’. This was founded in 18o6 and undertook a great deal of jobbing work besides the two important publications mentioned in the previous chapter: Albrecht Dürers christlich-mythologische Handzeichnungen (1808)² and the Musterbuch über alle lithographischen Kunstmanieren (1808).³ The first of these contained lithographed facsimiles by J. N. Strixner of a Dürer self-portrait and of forty-three of the marginal drawings he made for the Prayer Book of the Emperor Maximilian, and proved the value of the process for reproducing works of art; the second, which included brief descriptions of twenty-four different ways of working on stone, together with specimens of some of them, showed something of its versatility both for making drawings and for commercial purposes.

Soon after Senefelder began working for the Steuer-Kataster-Kommission, however, he was forced to sell his own lithographic business, and in 181o it passed to J. G. Zeller and J. C. von Mannlich. Zeller, a printseller and stationer, does not appear to have taken a very active part in the business and sold his interests in it to J. Selb five years later, but Johann Christian von Mannlich, Court Painter, Director of the Royal Museums and Galleries, and an important figure in Munich, did much to encourage the growth of lithography there by virtue of his position alone.⁴ As a gallery director Mannlich was a great educator with a real mission to promote an understanding and appreciation of the German schools of painting, and it was obviously with this in mind that he directed the publication of two sets of lithographed reproductions. The first of these, Les Oeuvres lithographiques. Contenant un choix de dessins d’ après les grands maîtres de toutes les écoles, tiré des Musées de sa Majesté le Roi de Bavière (Munich, 1810–16), was published by Strixner, Piloty et Compagnie in seventytwo monthly parts each containing six lithographs.² Mannlich supervised the publication, chose the works to be reproduced, and even drew sixteen of them on the stone himself, but nearly all the remainder were lithographed by J. N. Strixner and F. Piloty. The plates vary considerably in both size and style and an attempt was made to capture the spirit of the originals by using either crayon or pen and ink, or occasionally both combined. Most of the plates are printed with an additional tint stone, and some with two, and the success of this publication set the style for tinted lithography all over Europe. The second publication, Königlich Bayerischer Gemälde-Saal zu München und Schleissheim, began to appear in 1817 as soon as the other work was completed. Two hundred paintings were reproduced, mainly by tinted lithography, but the plates were on the whole larger and more ambitious than those for the Oeuvres lithographiques. Piloty and Strixner again lithographed the pictures, but this time with the help of Jacob von Dorner, Dominicus and Lorenz Quaglio, and many others. These two publications were probably the most important lithographed works produced in Germany during the first part of the nineteenth century. As examples of tinted lithography they were far superior to anything that had been produced before, and they had a wide influence by virtue of their subject matter. In this respect as well they are important since they represent the first successful application of lithography to the reproduction of a collection of works of art.³

The other early presses in Munich mentioned by Senefelder⁴ all seem to have been mainly commercial undertakings. Mettenleiter set up a press at the Königliche unmittelbare Steuer-Kataster-Kommission', which Senefelder himself took over in 18o9. This institution was principally concerned with printing maps and plans, and was the most important lithographic printing works in Munich; about thirty engravers were employed there and maps were printed in editions of 15,000–20,000 for taxation purposes. Theobald Senefelder was appointed to direct the lithographic press established at the Royal Foundations Office, where he was allowed to take on work for other government offices and even private clients; Winter set up a press for the use of the Róyal Privy Council; and Sidler, who began by printing for the Central State Pay Office, later turned to printing music and other commercial work on his own account. The other presses Senefelder mentions as having been working in Munich in this period were the lithographic office established at the Royal ‘Armen-Anstalt auf dem Anger', and the presses of Dietrich and Helmle and Roth.

While Munich was certainly the main centre for lithography in Germany, the mass of ordinary jobbing printing done there appears to have hindered its use by artists for making original drawings on stone. In this respect Offenbach and Berlin deserve special mention. At Offenbach, Johann André told his cousin, François Johannot, about the process and the latter set up a press which was used at first largely for the printing of music and various kinds of jobbing work.¹ By 18o4, however, Johannot had begun to print artists' lithographs, and amongst those who produced drawings at his press were Matthias Koch, Nicolaus Vogt, Prince Karl von Isenburg, Susemihl the younger, and Wilhelm Reuter. Reuter also worked in Berlin, and he is perhaps the most important early German lithographer, for besides producing some very fine lithographs himself he attracted many other artists to the process.2 Reuter (1768-1834) was a miniature and portrait painter by profession, and was one of the first German artists to draw on stone. According to his own testimony he began making experiments for himself in Berlin in 1798, soon after hearing about Senefelder's invention. His earliest lithographs were produced in 18o1, but his real enthusiasm for the process dates from 18o3 when he undertook a trip to Paris which enabled him to find out more about lithography in other parts of Europe. Apart from seeing what was being done in Paris itself, Reuter saw some prints from the English Specimens of polyautography while he was there, and on his outward journey he stopped at Darmstadt and met the lithographic printer François Johannot. This last event is of particular interest, for Reuter seems to have been one of Johannot's first recruits in his attempt to encourage artists to make drawings on stone. When Johannot returned to Offenbach he sent Reuter two lithographic stones already prepared for drawing, one grained and the other polished, and in a letter dated 17 July 1803 gave him simple practical instructions in the art of drawing on stone.' These contacts with other lithographers must have kindled Reuter's enthusiasm for the new process, and in the following year he invited a number of artists to contribute to the Polyautographische Zeichnungen vorzüglicher Berliner Künstler. Besides Reuter himself, many of the leading artists of Berlin made lithographs for the publication, including the neo-classical sculptor Johann Gottfried Schadow, until it finally lapsed in 18o8. Years later Reuter planned a similar but more ambitious undertaking, Polyautographische Zeichnungen der vorzüglichsten europäischen Künstler unseres Zeitalters, to demonstrate the various applications of the process. A start was made with a tinted lithograph by Wolf in 1818, but the series was never completed. Reuter himself continued to make lithographs until 1824, though scarcely improving on the strength and freedom of his early work.

By 182o lithography was being widely used in Germany for a variety of jobbing work such as circular letters, diagrams, and statistical tables, and for map and music printing; it was used occasionally too for items such as visiting cards and book covers and for producing facsimiles of both text and illustrations from old books. Lithography had also become firmly established as a suitable medium for the draughtsman—for original drawing and for making illustrations for books, topographical and portrait prints, and copies of paintings and drawings of the old masters. By the time Senefelder's treatise was published in 1818 the techniques of pen and chalk drawing and engraving on stone had all been brought to a competent level. In particular, the two techniques which were later to become the stock-in-trade of professional lithographers in England and France were being successfully practised. The first of these was the technique of building up tones in chalk lithography by a careful and systematic method of hatching, which can be seen in works such as the Lithographische Kunstprodukte and the Oeuvres litho-

1 The letter is printed in full by Reuter, op. cit., pp. 147–8. See below p. 73.

graphiques. The second was the technique of using one or more tint stones, often with the lights removed from them, which was employed to such good effect in the Oeuvres lithographiques and the Bayerischer Gemälde-Saal. In both these techniques Germany certainly provided the model for those early English and French lithographers who began to study the new process seriously.

For the most part, lithography in Germany followed the path mapped out for it by its inventor. The emphasis from the beginning was on the commercial and industrial ápplications of the process, and these continued to be the dominant influences on the development of lithography there throughout the nineteenth century. The practice of making cheap imitations of intaglio printing by etching or engraving on stone, which continued in Germany long after it had been abandoned in England and France, and the superiority of German craftsmen in chromolithography later on in the century are symptomatic of this attitude. The lithographic products for which Germany became noted were in the very same fields that had attracted the attention of the first lithographers—music printing and the reproduction of works of art; but as far as the development of the original lithographic print was concerned her contribution virtually ended with the rise of French lithography in the 182os.

# LITHOGRAPHY IN ENGLAND 1801-1818

NGLAND WAS THE FIRST COUNTRY OUTSIDE THE GERMAN states to have a lithographic press. As early as the winter of 18oo Senefelder travelled to London with a view to obtaining the exclusive right to use his process in this country, and on 20 June 18o1 a patent was granted for A new method and process of performing the various branches of the arts of printing on paper, linen, cotton, woollen and other articles.² The idea was obviously to cover the use of the process for the printing of fabrics, a field which Johann André had himself explored only a short while before in connection with Senefelder's proposed stone-cylinder machine, but in fact the very first use of lithography in England was for the multiplication of original drawings. While in London Senefelder stayed with Philipp André,³ one of Johann's younger brothers, and he complains bitterly in his treatise that he was kept ‘in a perfect seclusion from society, for fear of losing the secret' and that consequently his stay ‘had not the expected success with respect to establishing a lithographical office there'.' But this was not strictly true for, as Man has pointed out,2 a passage in the original edition of the treatise refers to some chalk lithographs done by the Swiss artist Gessner while Senefelder was still in England. Certainly by the time he returned to Germany in the summer of 18o1 a press of some kind existed in London.

The original patents for England and Scotland filed in Senefelder's name were yielded to Johann André by an agreement dated 10 August 18o1,³ and it was his agent in London, Philipp André, who was responsible for promoting the first lithographs produced in this country. André had been instructed in the practice of lithography by Senefelder while he was still in London, and soon after the patent had been secured he began to explain the process to artists and to encourage them to visit his printing room. The result of all this was a publication of artists’ prints called Specimens of polyautography, the first number of which was published 30 April 1803.4 The original intention was to issue six parts, each consisting of six lithographs and costing half a guinea, but André did not stay in London long enough to see it realized. Many of the leading artists of the day contributed, including Benjamin West, then President of the Royal Academy, Academicians Henry Fuseli, Thomas Stothard, and James Barry, the Swiss artist Conrad Gessner, and Thomas Barker of Bath. The first of these lithographs to bear a date was drawn by Benjamin West in 18o1, and it is tempting to suppose that he was used as a bait to attract other artists to the new process. Like most of the earlier lithographs in the Specimens his was a simple pen and ink study drawn on the stone just as if it had been paper. Chalk lithographs were much more difficult to print, especially in large numbers, and it is possibly for this reason that they are rarely found amongst the incunabula of English lithography.⁵ Both Gessner⁶ and Benjamin West7 had made chalk drawings on stone, and some of these are dated as early as 18o1, but they were never published. It was not until 18o4 that two chalk lithographs by Chalon and Gessner were drawn for the Specimens of polyautography.

By the autumn of 1803 two parts of the Specimens had already appeared, and these represent the first attempt anywhere to publish a collection of original artists' lithographs. It was seen both in this country and abroad as an interesting experiment and even inspired a similar publication in Germany.² How far André progressed with the publication of his Specimens of polyautography before he left England cannot yet be decided with any degree of certainty, but in any case he must have been responsible for printing many other lithographs as well. Some of the early English lithographs in the polyautographic collection of the British Museum³ certainly belong to this period, and must therefore have been printed by André before he returned to Germany in 1805, as his was the only press known to have been working at the time.

In 18o6, after a lapse of eighteen months, André was succeeded as patentee by G. J. Vollweiler, who had previously been an assistant to Johann André in Offenbach.4 Vollweiler reprinted the first two parts of the Specimens in 1806 and continued the series to its completion in 1807.5 With the fourth part he issued a circular6 in which he announced that he had taken over the press previously directed by André and that he intended to continue with the work. It is not clear therefore who supervised the drawings of the third part of the Specimens, but preparations for this and perhaps some of part four as well must have been under way when Vollweiler took over, and he must have inherited some of the stones with the drawings already on them.' Vollweiler is known to have taken over some of the drawn stones, for in addition to inviting new artists to contribute to the publication he reprinted the lithographs of the first two parts. Whether for want of subscribers² or because he found it difficult to get artists to patronize the new process,³ Vollweiler returned home discouraged in August 1807.

The various parts of the Specimens of polyautography published first by André and then by Vollweiler, though the most important early application of lithography in this country, were by no means all that appeared before the end of 1807. Besides the thirty-six prints of the polyautographic albums Man lists well over 1oo lithographs produced under the direction of André and Vollweiler. Amongst those artists who produced more than an occasional drawing on stone were Richard Cooper,4 drawing-master at Eton, who specialized in landscapes; the engraver Charles Heath, who made figure studies in pen and ink;⁵ and Conrad Gessner, who was one of the first to experiment with lithographic crayon and produced a number of drawings of animals in this medium before he returned to Switzerland in 18o4.6 These, however, are the exceptions, and most artists seem to have produced only one or two lithographs as something of an experiment.

Particularly important as an indication of future trends in lithography was the drawing made on stone by the antiquary and topographical draughtsman J. T. Smith for his own book The antiquities of Westminster (London, 18o7).7 The plate, a rather weak pen drawing in the style of an etching, is the first known instance of a lithograph being used to illustrate a book. The original intention was to illustrate the whole edition with one plate only produced by lithography; but the process was obviously not quite so easy as it seemed, and after 3oo prints had been taken the stone was ruined and it was decided to revert to etching on copper for the remaining copies. The first 3oo copies of the edition have both the lithographed and etched versions as Smith decided to use his failure as an opportunity to describe the process.

Besides this isolated and unsuccessful use of lithography for the illustration of a book, one well-known artist drew on stone with apparently no view to publication. William Blake, who was a subscriber to Smith's book,2 and whose interest may even have been aroused by reading the description of the process there, made a pen drawing on stone in about 18o7 which is generally known as Job in Prosperity'.³ A number of amateurs also made lithographs in this period, the most distinguished being the Duc de Montpensier, younger brother of Louis-Philippe, who produced several chalk drawings on stone, dated 1805 and 1806, while he was staying in England at Twickenham.4 But there are also scores of miscellaneous sketches in the polyautographic collection of the British Museum by artists and amateurs who will probably never be identified, and some at least must have escaped the attention of Thomas Fisher who brought them together at the beginning of the century.

The contribution of the antiquary Fisher⁵ as a collector, draughtsman, and publicist of lithography has not been sufficiently appreciated, though it was partly due to his encouragement that the process was kept alive in England after Vollweiler's departure. It is not known how early he became interested in lithography, but in 18o7 he made a number of drawings on stone of some fresco paintings he had found three years earlier on the walls of the Chapel of the Trinity at Stratford on Avon.' The lithographs were drawn by Fisher himself with a pen and were printed by Vollweiler in an edition of 12o copies, after which the drawings were erased from the stones.² The first two parts, consisting of plates without text, were published in 1807 with the title A series of antient allegorical, historical, and legendary paintings . . . on the walls of the Chapel of the Trinity at Stratford upon Avon in Warwickshire.3 The title-page itself was lithographed and this and the fifteen reproductions were coloured by hand. Before the third part could be published Vollweiler had returned to Germany, and all the remainder of the plates were engraved on copper except one, a large representation of the Painting of the Resurrection and Day of Judgement' (pl. xix) which occupies a double spread. This was lithographed in the style of the earlier prints and is lettered ‘Drawn 1804, Etched and Published January the 1st. 1808 by Thos. Fisher, Hoxton'. Since Fisher himself records that Vollweiler left the country in the August of the previous year⁴ we can only assume that Fisher printed this plate with the help of Vollweiler's former assistant, D. J. Redman.¹

Fisher is also recorded as having used lithography in two other publications,² but for his major work on the county of Bedfordshire, begun in 1811 and lasting till the year of his death, he chose to use copper-engraving and aquatint.³ After Vollweiler's departure Fisher seems to have been more concerned with bringing the process before a wider public than with producing lithographs himself. In March 18o8 he wrote an article on ‘The process of polyautographic printing' which appeared in the Gentleman's magazine,⁴ and in which he pleaded for a revival of the process before the patent expired. Again in October 1815 he contributed a short note to the same periodical⁵ to accompany a specimen portrait drawn by Henry Winkworth and printed by Redman, and in this he took the opportunity of advertising the lithographic press which Redman had recently set up in London. Though Fisher continued to take an interest in lithography,6 he seems to have taken no further active part in promoting the process after 1815.

Until Vollweiler's departure lithography in England had been almost exclusively devoted to the multiplication of drawings by artists and amateurs,7 many of which found their way into Fisher's collection. The only indication that commercial work might have been undertaken is a claim which Vollweiler made in one of his own lithographed circulars for the Specimens of polyautography that the process had been considerably improved by its application to letter- and music-printing, of which the present advertisement, as well as the title page, and the enclosed sheet of music are specimens."8 In this respect the course lithography followed in England for the first few years was very different from the pattern established in Germany, where it was mainly used for commercial purposes. But when Vollweiler returned to Germany in 18o7 lithography took on a very different direction in England, for he sold the secret of the process and some of the materials to the Quarter-Master-General's Office in Whitehall where it was to be used for official and military purposes. At first little progress was made, the officer who had been initiated into the principles of the process being unable to make it work satisfactorily. It was only when Redman was discovered and persuaded to assist at the Horse Guards that they managed to print with reasonable success. D. J. Redman, who had previously worked for André, Vollweiler, and perhaps Fisher as well, was the first English lithographic printer, and it was with his help that experiments were made with a view to printing the kind of work that had already been produced in Germany during the previous few years. At this stage Redman was by no means a qualified printer, but he had picked up a smattering of the process while engaged as an assistant. Nevertheless, he began experimenting at the Horse Guards and the first successful production of the press, a plan of Bantry Bay, was published on 7 May 18o8. Other jobs of this kind soon followed, as well as circulars and diagrams, but it was not until a new Quarter-Master-General was appointed in 1811 that the lithographic press in Whitehall really began to flourish. The quality of the printing done there during the first few years was not particularly high, and could certainly not compare with similar work produced in Germany in the same period, but it gradually improved and lithography continued to be used successfully at the Horse Guards for the printing of maps, plans, and circulars for many years.²

To this same period (1808–12) belong the eight illustrations which James Forbes drew on stone and included as something of an experiment in his Oriental memoirs. They were rather coarsely printed by Redman in 1811 and 1812 and compare most unfavourably with the engravings and aquatints of the rest of the publication, but this was, all the same, an important venture in book illustration.² Artists continued to be attracted to the process, but far fewer than before: the brothers Cornelius and John Varley³ are known to have made a number of lithographs between 1808 and 1811, P. S. Munn produced a drawing book with lithographed sketches in 181o,⁴ and a few other isolated prints date from this time. Besides Forbes's illustrations Redman printed at least one of Cornelius Varley's lithographs (dated 181o)⁵ and it must be assumed that he was responsible for all those printed in this period, even though it is difficult to see quite how he could have done so while employed at the Horse Guards.6

Until 1812 there seems to have been only one set of lithographic equipment in England, for the press set up by Senefelder passed successively from André to Vollweiler and then to the Quarter-Master-General's Office. But at the close of 1812 or the beginning of 1813 Redman decided to branch out on his own and set up the first English lithographic press outside London at the fashionable city of Bath. No doubt the work he had been doing at the Horse Guards was thoroughly menial compared with the printing he had previously done with André and Vollweiler, and at Bath he directed his attention principally to the printing of artists' drawings. While there he came into contact with Bankes, who wrote and published the first English treatise on lithography at Bath in 1813.1 It is reasonable to suppose that it was Redman who instructed Bankes in the technical side of the process, for he is described in the text as being ‘under the patronage of the artists there, and at the service of the public, to provide the necessary materials, viz. the ink and pencil, and to prepare the stone, and take the impressions from drawings made on it'.² Moreover, it was probably through Redman that Bankes obtained some of the original stones of the Specimens of polyautography, for once again there is a suggestion that these should be reissued.3 With the removal of Redman from London, Bath was now the centre of lithography in England—at least for the printing of artists' drawings—and all the important productions for the next three years were printed by Redman there. One of his principal clients was the artist Thomas Barker, better known as Barker of Bath, who published two sets of his own drawings which are the first important‘one man' collections oflithographs to be issued in England. In 1813 appeared Forty lithographic impressions of rustic figures4 and in the following year Thirty two lithographic impressions of landscape scenery.s Both were drawn in the pen and ink style and were printed by Redman. From the same period (1813-15) date Eight lithographic impressions6 and a number of other prints by various amateur artists of Bath,' but otherwise there is little record of Redman's activities there. Such publications would certainly not have been sufficient to keep Redman and his family for three years, and with the experience of the Quarter-Master-General's press behind him we must assume that he did some jobbing printing as well. All the same, after the initial enthusiasm of artists had worn off, the prospect could not have looked too promising, and by October 1815 Redman was back in London.²

For three years, therefore, Bath was the centre of lithography in England.³ It was at Bath that the first English treatise on the process was published and where it was first called lithography; the first attempts to find a suitable English stone to replace those imported from Germany were made in the limestone hills around Bath; and, excepting the Specimens of polyautography, most of the important early lithographed publications were issued there. But after Redman's return both the lithographic presses known to have been working at the time were in London, and for many years to come all the major developments took place in the capital

Redman appears to have been no more successful in London than he was in Bath.4 An article in the Gentleman's magazine⁵ accompanying a specimen of lithography mentions his assistance with some of Ackermann's publications, but points out that the specimen is included partly to further the interests of this poor but industrious man' who lives solely by his art. Despite this rather pathetic picture we have of Redman he continued printing until at least the end of 1823, producing plates for a minimum of five topographical volumes6 besides illustrations for The Freebooter.7 In 1819 Redman was awarded a Silver Isis medal by the Society of Arts for a lithograph executed on English stone. He was primarily a printer of pen and ink work; some of his chalk printing was very poor, and even the best of it could not compare with work produced by either the first generation of printers in England or by those who were to follow him. Nevertheless, he was largely responsible for keeping lithography alive in England after Vollweiler's departure, and he served as a link between the very first lithographic printers and the master technicians of the next decade

The real growth of lithography in this country was delayed until contacts with Germany were once again established. For ten years following Vollweiler's departure in 1807 England had been completely cut off from the continent as far as lithography was concerned, and Redman and his successors at the Quarter-Master-General's Office must have continued to practise the process almost as it was known when first introduced to England at the beginning of the century.² An examination of the lithographs produced in this period shows in fact a steady decline in the quality of the printing, and especially in the printing of chalk drawings,³ from the Specimens of polyautography onwards. Even Fisher in his recommendation of Redman in the Gentleman's magazine admitted that he had not produced works of such delicacy and fineness as his predecessors.4 It was not until peaceful conditions were once again established in Europe after the Napoleonic Wars that advantage could be taken of the improvements made in Munich by Senefelder, Mitterer, and Mannlich; and it so happened that this coincided exactly with the expiration of the English patent.

The growth and spread of lithography in this country nearly twenty years after its first introduction was mainly due to two men who had both visited Senefelder in Munich—Rudolph Ackermann, the well-known publisher of aquatint books, and Charles Hullmandel, an artist. Ackermann was largely responsible for bringing lithography to the notice of the English public, while Hullmandel turned his attention more to the practical issues of improving printing methods and teaching artists to draw on stone.

A German by birth, Ackermann settled in London and in 1795 opened a print shop in the Strand which soon became a fashionable centre for the arts in Georgian England. There, at the Repository of Arts, he ran a drawing school and later opened an art library and held literary evenings. But besides being a successful publisher and patron of the arts Ackermann was always interested in novelties, and one of the many inventions which attracted his attention was the process of lithography. Ackermann's interest in the process went right back to the beginning of the century when he published lithographs which had been printed for him by André² and Vollweiler.³ A contemporary notice in the Englische Miscellen even claimed that he wanted to buy the English patent.4 Little more is known of his early interest in lithography, but Fisher recorded in both 1808⁵ and 181 56 that he made use of it in some of his publications. Ackermann may have decided, as Man has suggested,7 to bide his time until the reaction of the public proved more favourable, but from this point of view he could hardly have chosen a less opportune time to set up a press. When he began printing, probably right at the beginning of 1817,8 lithography was at its lowest ebb in England, and perhaps this was one of the reasons why Ackermann, an astute publisher, began to encourage the process in the country.9 One of the first and most important productions of Ackermann's press, a further facsimile edition of Dürer's marginal drawings for the Emperor Maximilian's Prayer Book,' provides ample proof of his awareness of what was going on in Germany, and this is supported by an article accompanying the first lithograph in his Repository of arts in which he admits to trying to emulate the productions of Munich presses.²

In the year following the establishnent of his press Ackermann visited Senefelder in Germany, probably with a view to making arrangements for the publication of an English edition of the inventor's treatise. This appeared in 1819, but with the plates printed at Ackermann's own press as the import duty on prints from abroad would have made it too expensive for Senefelder to supply impressions from the original stones.³ Only a month after publication, on 6 April 1819, Ackermann presented a copy of the treatise to the Society of Arts and demonstrated the process before the Committee of Polite Arts on a small press sent by Senefelder himself.4 He also recommended that the Society should bestow an award on the inventor, and at a meeting of the Society held on the following day it was agreed that Senefelder should be presented with their Gold Medal.⁵ It was Ackermann, then, who really made the Society aware of the importance of the process which had already been practised in England for nearly twenty years,6 and, through the publication of Senefelder's treatise, brought it before an even wider public.
