The comparative cost of lithographic and copper-plate printing will be considered more fully later, but one instance is mentioned here to show that economic factors were important in determining the type of printing done from stone. For as long as the lithographic stone offered a cheap and reasonably effective substitute for the copper plate, and to a lesser extent for the wood block, it would continue to be used as such, and techniques appropriate to mechanical methods of printing would continue to be applied to a process which was essentially chemical.

In 181o Marcel de Serres wrote another article on lithography, Note sur divers procédés peu connus pour l’impression lithographique sur papier, sur toile ou sur étoffes', which was published in Annales des arts et manufactures. This time he addressed his note not from the lithographic backwater of

Vienna, where most of the printing had been commercial, but from Munich which was still the centre for all kinds of lithographic printing. All the important improvements to the process had been made in Munich, and although plenty of commercial printing was undertaken by its numerous presses, artists also drew on stone there with both pen and crayon. Marcel de Serres was consequently in a position to form a much clearer and more accurate impression of the process than he had been able to in Vienna.

Nearly half the article is concerned with a method of printing fabrics by means of stone cylinders; but the remainder is almost entirely devoted to an account of the planographic branch of lithography, and it is worth noting that the new name is adopted for the title. There is a passing reference to engraving on stone, but the emphasis is on drawing with either a pen or brush. Marcel de Serres shows a far greater understanding of the process here than in his previous article. His description of the method of preparing the stone and printing from it is convincing; he is able to give a recipe for the composition of the drawing ink, and for the first time mentions the use of gum arabic. He even explains in some detail a method of transferring drawings done on paper that has been previously coated with a special gummy solution. Above all Marcel de Serres realizes, and states unequivocally, where the real advantage of the process lay:

Ce que la lithographie a vraiment de particulier, c’est qu’on peut, par un procédé fort simple, obtenir un très-grand nombre d'épreuves, non-seulement d’une gravure qui est faite sur la pierre, mais simplement d’un dessin qu’on y a tracé, soit au pinceau, soit à la plume . . . il suffit de savoir dessiner.

This reflects an entirely different attitude to the art of lithography, and anticipates a series of articles that Marcel de Serres contributed to the Annales des arts et manufactures during the first half of 1814, in which the principles of lithography are clcarly and fully stated. These articles will be considered in the section dealing with the early treatises where, in terms of scale, they more properly belong.

This survey of the early literature of lithography has taken us to 181o, the year in which the first independcnt treatise on the process was published in Germany. It may not be a complete account of these notices, but it is comprehensive enough to make it clear that up till 181o the process remained a closely guarded secret. There seems to have been a real interest in the process, and the periodicals at least attempted to give some coherent explanation of how it worked, and yet none of the notices we have considered so far can be regarded as useful guides to those wanting to try lithography for themselves. For the most part they give only vague directions which are often misleading and sometimes inaccurate. Yet by this time (181o) some competent pieces of lithographic printing had been produced in Germany, France, and England. We can only assume that it was not through ignorance of the process alone that it remained a mystery; but that to a large extent this was due to the lithographic printers themselves who, once they had discovered the secret of the process, jealously guarded it. We shall find ample evidence for this assumption in the introductions to the first books devoted to lithography—the early treatises.

# TREATISES PRIOR TO SENEFELDER'S

NLY A FEW TREATISES APPEARED BEFORE SENEFELDER'S MANUAL, and these were primarily concerned with doing some pioneering work and making the process known. In general they are smaller in scope and scale than most of the later treatises, and at least some of them seem to have been unknown by the next generation of printers. During this period lithography continued to be a subject of interest in the periodicals, and the reader is referred to the bibliographies for the more important of these articles.

The very first treatise on lithography was published in Germany with the title Das Geheimniss des Steindrucks. It appeared in two editions, both dated 181o, one published by Cotta in Tübingen and the other by Giegler in Schweinfurt (Plates 2, 3). From the introduction to the Tübingen edition it appears that the author of this first treatise was Heinrich Rapp, but his name was not revealed on the title-page of either version; in the Tübingen edition he is called ‘ein Liebhaber' and in the Schweinfurt edition ‘ein Kunstfreund'. Little is known about Rapp, except that he is supposed to have learned about the process from Charles Strohofer when the latter set up a press in Stuttgart in the spring of 1807 and advertised his services to artists and amateurs. In the same year Rapp supplied a chalk lithograph to accompany the article on stone-printing in the Morgenblatt für gebildete Stände, and according to some early sources he became the first manager of the lithographic establishment which was set up in Stuttgart by the publishing firm of Cotta.

1Most of these treatises run to only twenty or thirty pages and only two of them exceed fifty pages.

Both editions of Rapp's treatise are slim quartos, but the one published at Tübingen is more comprehensive and includes a short history of the lithographic establishment at Stuttgart which, along with other parts, was not printed in the Schweinfurt edition. The Tübingen version also has more plates and illustrates a greater range of methods. The dates 18o7 and 1808 are found on some of the plates of this edition, at least four of which were drawn by Rapp himself; in the Schweinfurt edition all the dated plates were drawn in 181o and five were signed by Seiler. Everything points to the fact that the Tübingen edition was the earlier of the two. It certainly seems to have been far better known, and was the one referred to by Marcel de Serres,² Peignot,3 Senefelder,4 and Engelmann.⁵

The title of Rapp's treatise, Das Geheimniss des Steindrucks, is in itself indicative of the state of lithography at the time, and the author explains in the introduction that while much labour and expense had been bestowed upon the art ‘The greatest disadvantage of all was the thick veil which was spread over the whole thing under the pretext of secrecy and which was guarded most jealously for a long time.'6 Many had tried to better themselves financially by offering the secret for sale and so it was inevitable, he continues, that ‘small disclosures should also occur which conveyed to the public something of the form and nature of these unknown things under their veil of secrecy'.7 Such people had done much to make the basic idea known, but they had not helped to make it understood; and, in the absence of a description by Senefelder, Rapp felt obliged to write one himself:

The author has a special right to share his views and discoveries with the world, and it is only out of modesty that he has withheld them until now, because he would willingly have left the initial step to the first inventor. But since it seems more and more uncertain as time goes on whether the latter will ever express himself publicly, it really becomes a duty to make it known.

He points out in the introduction that stone-printing should not be regarded as a new branch of an already existing art, but as something entirely new; though he admits that the perfection of the established processes had made it almost impossible to believe that any other method of printing could exist. It was the very simplicity of the new process, Rapp claims, that prevented it from being understood, and he states clearly, and apart from Senefelder's specification almost certainly for the first time, that lithography depends on two basic principles. The first of these was that most kinds of stone absorb both water and greasy fluids, and the second that these two fluids oppose one another:In these ordinary phenomena lies the true and most inportant basis for the secret of stone-printing.’

The rest of the treatise is devoted to explaining in a practical way how to produce a lithograph. Rapp tells where the stones can be obtained and how to grain and polish them, gives recipes for the ink and chalk and instructions for drawing with them on both stone and paper. He provides illustrations of two presses and describes carefully how to prepare the stones and print from them. Good specimens of the various methods of drawing on stone are supplied with both editions (Plates 4-1o). This is the model, though small in scale, for most lithographic treatises which appeared later.

Even though the author sees lithography as a completely new process, he panders to tradition by explaining the process of engraving with the burin on stone and methods of imitating aquatints and woodcuts, and he writes at length about the advantages of transferring copper-engravings to stone. All the same, emphasis is put on the planographic aspects of the process, and Rapp's instructions are simple and eminently practical. His description of lithography is the first that could have been understood and followed by the complete beginner, and the hope he expressed right at the end of his treatise, that with its help ‘everyone who wants to can produce prints just as I do', was fully borne out in practice by the experience of Engelmann who, apparently with this book alone, managed to build a press and make some experimental lithographs.²

For a short while, at least, Das Geheimniss des Steindrucks seems to have been well known. Parts of it were reproduced in the Neues Magazin aller neuen Erfindungen,³ and a notice of its publication appeared in Archives des découvertes.4 It is listed in the early bibliographies of Marcel de Serres5 and Peignot,⁶ and it was also mentioned by Senefelder, who refers to it in his own treatise as ‘the first publication that exhibited the new art in the right point of view'.7

The next lithographic treatise to appear was published in England. It was not nearly so well known as Rapp's, and I have been able to trace only one reference to it in the literature of lithography before the last quarter of the nineteenth century. It was written by H. Bankes and published at Bath in 1813 with the title Lithography; or, the art of making drawings on stone, for the purpose of being multiplied by printing (Plate 11). A second edition appeared anonymously in London in 1816 with the title slightly changed, Lithography; or, the art of taking impressions from drawings and writing iade on stone.8 The texts of the two editions differ slightly and the London version has more plates. The author's name does not appear on either title-page, but is given at the end of the text in the case of the 1813 edition.

Like Rapp, Bankes was an amateur artist. Four of his own pen lithographs have been preserved in the polyautographic albums assembled by Fisher, and it may be assumed that he also drew some of the plates for one or both editions of his treatise. Bankes was probably first instructed in the process by Redman when the latter moved to Bath, and his description of preparing the stone and printing from it appears to have been based on his observation of Redman at work rather than on his own experience.

The treatise begins with a short historical introduction in which Bankes explains that he is principally concerned with sponsoring the process as a branch of the arts and that he cannot consider the use of it in the Quarter-Master-General's Office, at the Horse-Guards, for the printing of plans of battles and maps of the seat of war, any application of it favorable to its encouragement as a branch of the fine arts . . .'.² He sees the greatest opportunities for lithography in the multiplication of original drawings ‘and it is principally to excite the attention of the public to this use of the art that the present communication is made ...'.³ With this in mind Bankes adopted the new word lithography, which is used here for the first time and is explained in the text:

Previous to my entering more minutely into the practice of the art, I shall notice its more prominent and obvious capacity, viz. that of multiplying the drawing from the hand of the master. It was with reference to this capacity, that Mr. P. H. André introduced the art, under the title of Polyautography, and intended to apply it to all the purposes of engraving. I have taken the liberty, however, to change this for Lithography, with a view to confine the invention to what is strictly denominated the Fine Arts, or drawing as the first branch of them.⁴

The term lithographie had been in use some time prior to this on the continent,5 and it may well be that Bankes had some knowledge of either French or German literature on the process.

On the other hand, Bankes makes no mention at all of the complicated variants to the process that were employed in Germany and described in the journals; for him lithography was purely planographic. He gives instructions for the preparation of polished and grained stones, supplies recipes for both ink and chalk, and describes simply and clearly how the stone is prepared and printed:

After the drawing is completed on the stone, it is necessary to place it over a trough of water, and pour over it a very slightly acidulated water, (by means of the nitric

3 Ibid., p. II.

acid) which will, but to an almost imperceptible degree, lower the surface of the stone where free from the tracings of the drawing; a weak solution of gum arabic is then applied, which has the effect of closing the open pores, and keeping the stone in a proper state of moisture for printing from. Thus prepared, the stone is taken to the press, and being washed over with water, the printer's dabber is applied, with the ink made extempore of lampblack, previously well ground, and linseed-oil prepared as for copper-plate ink. This ink being also greasy is resisted by the watered parts of the stone, but received by the tracings, upon which no water can lie; and being thus filled with ink, damp paper is laid over the stone, the pressure of a rolling or screw press is applied, and an impréssion taken. The process of watering, dabbing with ink, &c. is then repeated, another impression is taken, and so on for any number required.¹

The instructions are, however, mainly directed at the artist,² who is told that he must be careful to remove all dust from the stone before starting, must never allow his hand to touch it, and whether working with pen or chalk must make sure that the effect of gradating tints is obtained by the breadth of lines and not by their strength of tone. Greater attention is paid throughout the treatise to drawing with the pen. Bankes recommends a crow-quill for general use, but mentions that a hair pencil-brush or steel pen may be used for fine work. He suggests that the artist should keep pens of three thicknesses, one each for the distance, middle distance, and foreground, and he gives amateur artists who might like to practise the art for their own amusement the following advice:

. . . that they do not on any account give their drawings any thing like the character of engraving; this would cramp the hand, fetter the genius, and considerably diminish the value of the work: let them take the stone, when prepared, and, as if they were to draw on paper, proceed with the same freedom and spirit.³

But despite these recommendations Bankes ends his treatise with some suggestions for drawing landscapes with the pen which seem to be directly inspired by the syntax of copper-engraving and etching. Shadows, he recommends, ‘should be expressed by parallel straight lines which have no local character . . .' except in clouds where the shade tint ‘must be produced by parallel lines gently waving and deviating from the line of the horizon'.⁴

This first English treatise was, as the title suggests, directed principally at the artist. It must have been a useful handbook, describing all that the amateur might need to know in order to draw on stone, but it did not go into the chemical and technical aspects of the process in enough detail for it to have been of much use to the prospective lithographic printer. It may have aroused a certain amount of local interest in the new invention, but it cannot have been of any great value in helping to spread the art in this country, let alone abroad.

Mention must be made here of the series of articles which Marcel de Serres contributed to the Annales des arts et manufactures during the first half of 1814, for while these do not amount to an independent treatise, they are on the scale of one, running to over 15o pages. This is by far the longest account of the new process yet to have appeared, and the first outside Germany to explain its principles clearly. It is divided into two parts; the first deals with the history of the art and the principles on which it is founded, and the second with a description of the materials used and the methods of printing. Marcel de Serres mentions some of the accounts of lithography already published, but notes that:

Quel que soit le mérite de ces différens écrits, il m’a paru qu’il n’y en avait aucun qui rendît compte des procédés avec les détails qu’ils exigent, et qui fît connaître les principes généraux sur lequels ils sont fondés. C’est donc ce qui m’a déterminé à publier ces observations.²

He sees lithography as something quite different from the processes of relief and intaglio engraving, and states clearly the principles on which it is founded. First, that calcareous limestone imbibes water; secondly, that it attracts and absorbs greasy or resinous substances; and thirdly, that grease and resin have an affinity for their like and will repulse water and all damp bodies. These three principles, he continues, are the basis of all lithography, and from them it will follow that: (1) a greasy mark will adhere so strongly to the stone that it can only be removed by mechanical means; (2) all parts of the stone not covered with grease will absorb water; (3) if greasy colour is passed over the prepared stone the colour will attach itself to the grease and will be repelled by the damp stone. The purely planographic nature of the process is affirmed when the author tells us that the acid is only used to make the stone more capable of imbibing water. Despite all this he does not entirely abandon the use of the stone as a substitute for copper, and divides lithographic prints into two principal types: the gravure au tracé', or simple drawing on stone, and the ‘gravure entaillée', which involves working with the burin as for either copper-engraving or wood-engraving. But he leaves us in no doubt as to which is the more important of the two and the one with the greater possibilities:

La première est la seule qui donne ce résultat remarquable de faire obtenir des gravures autographes, à la plume, au crayon et au pinceau, de tout dessin quelconque.²

## and again:

C’est donc, on ne saurait trop le répéter, ce dernier genre de gravure [au tracé] qu'il faudrait chercher à étendre et à perfectionner plutôt que les autres procédés lithographiques, puisque des essais encore imparfaits ont déjà donné des résultats très-remarquables.³

The chapters relating to the techniques of lithography are the most useful and comprehensive yet to have appeared⁴ and, judging by the amount of detailed information given, Marcel de Serres must have been able to study the process very closely while in Munich and Vienna.5 He gives copious directions for the choice of stones, tells where to find them, and describes in detail how to prepare them for both engraving and for simple drawing with the crayon. He supplies recipes for drawing and printing inks, and good working instructions for printing from both engraved stones and simple drawings, explaining what to do, for instance, if the stone gets soiled or starts scumming up. These articles by Marcel de Serres were in fact much more complete in their instructions than any of the independent treatises published prior to Senefelder's own handbook; and through them he must have helped to dispel much of the mystery that still surrounded the process, for none of the early treatises can have reached such a wide public as the numbers of the Annales des arts et manufactures.

Apart from the second edition of Bankes's treatise, which was published in 1816, no other handbook on lithography appeared until 1818. But in the year that Senefelder published his own treatise three others appeared in Dijon, Paris, and Brussels. They were: M[airet], Notice sur la lithographie; D\*\*\*, Procédé actuel de la lithographie; and L. J. D. B., Coup-d'œil sur la lithographie.' The first of them was published in January 1818² and must therefore have been written before the appearance of Senefelder's treatise in Munich.³ The authors of the other two books seem to have had no knowledge of the inventor's treatise either, so it seems reasonable to discuss them here even though their manuals may have been published after Senefelder's.

In this connection it is worth noting that all three authors, like Rapp and Bankes before them, chose not to reveal their names on the title-pages of their treatises. Nothing is known of either L. J. D. B. or D\*\*\*. The first describes himself on the title-page as ‘Sténographe, Artiste-écrivain, et dessinateur', but the latter seems to have been an amateur⁴ for he explains in the preface to his treatise how he read about the process in the reports of various societies, obtained the necessary materials, and began to experiment for himself. The remaining author we know something about. He was F. Mairet, described by Peignot as a‘marchand papetier, relieur distingué’ who was responsible for setting up the second lithographic press at Dijon.⁵ Mairet was therefore the first professional lithographic printer to write a book about the process, as both Rapp and Bankes were primarily amateur artists.

In the preface to his treatise Mairet explains that he has only described the things he has tried out for himself and proved to be satisfactory. He is even rather apologetic about having to do so himself, and suggests that it would have been better if a man of letters like Lasteyrie had written this notice:

. . . mais voyant que jusqu'à ce jour les artistes et les amateurs étaient privés des connaissances nécessaires pour mettre en usage cet art qui peut leur être très-utile, je me suis hasardé à leur donner moi-même une méthode.²

But for all this Mairet must have been familiar with the series of articles on lithography in the Annales des arts et manufactures, for his description of the three basic principles and the three secondary consequences is almost identical to the classification adopted by Marcel de Serres.3

According to Peignot, who includes the work in his bibliography of 1819, Mairet's book was a tremendous success as soon as it was published owing partly to the importance of the subject, which the inventor had kept a mystery, and partly to the exactness and clarity with which it was treated:

On peut d’autant plus compter sur les renseignemens détaillés dans ce volume, que l’auteur n’a rien avancé qu’il n’ait exécuté lui-même, et qu’il n’ait jugé vrai, bon et exact, d'après les résultats satisfaisans qu'il a obtenus. Il peut donc en toute sûreté servir de guide aux artistes et aux amateurs, qui voudront s’occuper de Lithographie.4

The treatise is divided into three parts; the first deals with the theory of lithography, the second with the preparation of the materials and with the various ways of drawing on stone, and the third with the methods of printing and the presses used. Mairet describes three ways of making lithographs— using crayon, ink, or a method he calls dessin à la pointe'⁵ (Plates 13, 14)— and he explains in particular some of the precautions to be taken when working. There is no mention anywhere of using the stone as a substitute for copper by engraving into it with the burin and printing it intaglio. By omitting this method, which had been described in nearly all the earlier accounts of lithography, Mairet's treatise is considerably simplified and the basic principles of the process are more clearly seen. Though not nearly so comprehensive nor so thorough in its instructions as the account given by Marcel de Serres, it gains in clarity by being precise. Furthermore, it had the practical advantage of appearing as an independent work complete in one small (12mo) volume.

Mairet's book seems to have had an immediate success. In the following year it was translated into German, and a second French edition was published in 1824.² By contrast the works of D\*\*\* and L. J. D. B. attracted little attention even amongst contemporary writers. Neither is mentioned by Peignot, though his researches extended topersonnes auxquelles ce nouvel art est familier' and he was writing only a year after their appearance in the very city where one of them was published. D\*\*\*'s treatise was known to Ridolfi and Tartini, who wrote the first Italian handbook on lithography in the following year,3 and also by Engelmann who lists it in his bibliography,⁴ but I can find no reference at all to the book by L. J. D. B.⁵

Yet his Coup-d’æil sur la lithographie, produced within a year of the first press being set up in Brussels, is much the clearer of the two, and it is hard to understand why it should have escaped notice. It was intended mainly for the amateur artist and deals with both drawing on stone and printing from it in a very practical manner, even to the extent of explaining where lithographic stones, presses, chalks, and inks could be bought in Brussels. In addition, the author describes how the drawing materials and printing varnish are made, and carefully lists all the various stages in the preparation and printing of the stones. He writes about three ways of working on stone: drawing with chalk, writing with pen and ink either direct to stone or on to transfer paper, and engraving intaglio with a burin or steel point. Each of these methods is described separately, together with instructions for making corrections and preparing stones for printing.

By comparison D\*\*\*, Procédé actuel de la lithographie, is not nearly so well ordered nor so ample in its instructions, and fully deserved Engelmann's scathing criticism.' The author covers much the same ground as L. J. D. B. and mentions the same methods of working on stone, but in addition to describing how to engrave the stone for intaglio printing he also describes a process similar to Mairet's dessin à la pointe. There are many other similarities between the two treatises, and it seems very likely that the author of Procédé actuel de la lithographie had already studied Mairet's book before he wrote his own account of the process.²

Thus, by the end of 1818, separate treatises on lithography had been published in Germany, England, France, and Belgium, and even the later ones can, for all practical purposes, be said to have preceded Senefelder's Vollständiges Lehrbuch der Steindruckerey which appeared in the same year. The first Italian treatise was published a year later in 1819,³ some five years before the Italian translation of Senefelder's handbook,4 so that in many countries of western Europe the first serious accounts of the process came, not from the inventor, but from others who had mastered its principles only with varying degrees of success.

# SENEFELDER'S TREATISE

DENEFELDER WAS TO WAIT SOME TWENTY YEARS BEFORE PUBLISHING his own description of the process of lithography, despite the very real need for an authoritative manual. Early writers on lithography repeatedly complained that when they set up a press there was no handbook available to guide them.¹ Some, like the writer of the article in the Morgenblatt für gebildete Stände and Rapp in Das Geheimniss des Steindrucks, went so far as to hint that it was the inventor's job to fill this gap, and there can be little doubt that Senefelder was under continual pressure to write an account of his process. In 18o8 he did in fact begin a collection of specimens of the various manners of lithography, but the project was never completed, and though the text lists twenty-four different methods of working on stone, only one number was ever produced.² The specimens were meant to form part of a compendium which Senefelder expected would occupy his attention for many years, but as he explains in his treatise when it was eventually published, the art had made such progress ‘that what I thought a perfect print this year, did not seem so in the next . . .'.3 When in 181I he again took up the idea of publishing a treatise in conjunction with André it was intended that the work should be illustrated with drawings by the best artists of Frankfurt, but these asked treble the price of a similar engraving in copper, as they were not yet masters of the new art . . .’,1 and once again publication was postponed. Senefelder was essentially a practical man, and there can be little doubt that he must have been more concerned with perfecting his invention than describing it. Only when doubts arose in the press as to the true author of the invention was Senefelder finally goaded into writing his treatise.

In 1816 the date for publication was finally fixed and Senefelder began composing a description of all the different manners, and their management . . .'2 while a new sét of specimens was being prepared, this time by Munich artists. Once again progress was delayed because Senefelder was called away to set up a lithographic press in Vienna; and the treatise might perhaps never have been published but for the promptings of Friedrich von Schlichtegroll, director of the Royal Academy of Arts and Sciences at Munich. Allegations had begun to appear in the press that lithography was invented in Paris or London, and rumours which were circulating undermined Senefelder's position as inventor. In an effort to remove these doubts Schlichtegroll contributed a series of letters on lithography to the Anzeiger für Kunst- und Gewerbfleiss im Königreiche Baiern during the latter part of 1816 and the beginning of 1817.3 He had collected information on its invention and progress from local Munich people, including Senefelder's brothers and assistants, and called upon readers to correct any inaccuracies in his account. But above all he called upon Senefelder himself not to delay any longer publishing a history of his invention together with complete instructions for all the various branches of lithography. Schlichtegroll sent copies of these printed letters to Senefelder personally, and they seem to have had the desired effect, for Senefelder acknowledged that it was to Schlichtegroll's continual encouragement, that the present Treatise on Lithography owes its origin and completion'.4

The work was first published in Munich and Vienna in 1818 with the title Vollständiges Lehrbuch der Steindruckerey (Plate 12), and with a preface by Schlichtegroll. The book itself was not illustrated, but appeared with a supplement of twenty plates showing the various manners of lithography. It is some indication of the need for this manual that within a year of publication translations appeared in both England and France. Both were published in 1819, the French edition with the title L'Art de la lithographie and the English translation with the title A complete course of lithography. Later editions of the German work were published in 1821 and 1827, and an Italian translation appeared in 1824.1

The book is divided into two main parts; the first deals with the history of the invention and its different processes, and the second gives practical instructions for carrying out the various manners described. Compared with the early treatises it is a lengthy book. The German edition and the English translation of it are both quarto volumes and run into over three hundred pages. Everything is described in the minutest detail,'and it remained the most exhaustive treatise on lithography until Engelmann's Traité théorique et pratique de lithographie, which was published some twenty years later. The range of the treatise is enormous, and Senefelder describes a variety of processes like the ‘engraved' and ‘etched' manners which depend as much on differences of relief as on chemical principles, besides dealing with the purely planographic methods of simple chalk and ink drawing.

Yet Senefelder states quite clearly how his process differs from both intaglio and letterpress printing in the introduction to part two of the treatise:

Lithography is a branch of a new method of printing, differing in its fundamental principles from all other methods now in use, and known by the name of Chemical Printing. All the other methods of printing, hitherto known, might be divided into two branches; the one multiplying the original by elevated forms, the other by engraved forms.²

These, he explains, depend entirely on mechanical principles, whereas his method is quite new:

The chemical process of printing is totally different from both. Here it does not matter whether the lines be engraved or elevated; but the lines and points to be printed ought to be covered with a liquid, to which the ink, consisting of a homogeneous substance, must adherc, according to its chemical affinity and the laws of attraction, while, at the same time, all those places that are to remain blank, must possess the quality of repelling the colour. These two conditions, of a purely chemical nature, are perfectly attained by the chemical process of printing; for common experience shews that all greasy substances, such as oil, butter, &c., or such as are easily soluble in oil, as wax, bitumen, &c., do not unite with any watery liquid, without the intervention of a connecting medium; but that, on the contrary, they are inimical to water, and seem to repel it. The principal dissolving and uniting liquid for the above-mentioned substances is alcali, which, by proper management, forms a sort of soap, soluble in water.

Upon this expcrience rests the whole foundation of the new method of printing, which, in order to distinguish it from the mechanical methods, is justly called the chemical method; because the reason why the ink, prepared of a scbaceous matter, adheres only to the lines drawn on the plate, and is repelled from the rest of the wetted surface, depends entirely on the mutual chemical affinity, and not on mechanical contact alone.1

The novelty of his invention does not prevent Senefelder from seeing it as a rival to the other processes, particularly for commercial purposes. It had in his eyes the value of imitating very easily certain branches of copperplate and letterpress printing, while at the same time being capable of other methods, like the chalk and transfer manners, which were inimitable. It was clearly Senefelder's intention to promote his own invention and show its wide range and versatility. By mentioning all the many possibilities he hoped to show lithography as the most important of the graphic arts, even though he must have realized by this time that some of the manners he described were not very practicable. He gives no emphasis at all to the purely planographic part of lithography which within ten years of 1818 had more or less ousted the others. In this respect he is less prophetic than Marcel de Serres, who had pointed out in 1814 that this was likely to be the most important branch of lithography.²

What Senefelder succeeded in doing with the publication of this book was to establish himself surely as the inventor of the process, and to remove the veil of secrecy, which had shrouded it for twenty years, by supplying an informative book of reference for those who already knew something about the process. What it certainly did not do was supply an ideal introduction to anyone intending to take up lithography. This point was made by Houbloup in his Théorie lithographique of 1825, when he qualified his praise of Senefelder's treatise by pointing out that it was both too expensive and too complicated for an elementary work:

Depuis l’introduction de la lithographie en France, plusieurs ouvrages ont paru sur ce sujet. Le plus estimé est celui de M. SENEFELDER, son inventeur; mais son prix élevé s’oppose à ce qu’il soit à la portée de tout le monde; il est d’ailleurs trop compliqué pour un ouvrage élémentaire; et, en outre, les nombreux changemens qui ont été faits dans la partie matérielle de cet art, et qui tendent tous à le simplifier, ne permettent plus de puiser des règles invariables dans le traité dont il s’agit.1

In this chapter we are concerned with the second part of the treatise only, the part dealing with the practical applications of the process. This is divided into three main sections: 'General rules and explanations', 'Of the different manners of lithography', and a‘Supplement'. Though rather tedious these must be considered in some detail, as they provide something of a peg on which to hang subsequent developments of lithography.

In the first section Senefelder gives, in separate chapters, comprehensive descriptions of the materials used in lithography. To begin with he describes where the stones can be found, the qualities to look for, and how they should be grained and polished. In the chapter devoted to lithographic inks no fewer than eight differcnt recipes are supplied for both the drawing ink and the chalk, together with instructions for preparing them and additional recipes for etching grounds and inks for transferring.

The third chapter, on acids and other compositions used to prepare the stone, is particularly interesting because Senefelder stresses here the essential planographic quality of lithography by pointing out that the most important part of the preparation of the stone is not the etching but the application of gum arabic:

Most people who have occupied themselves with Lithography still maintain the opinion, which, for a long time, was my own, that the etching with aqua-fortis, or any other acid, prepares the stone, and that the subsequent application of gum only adds to it. But numerous experiments have now convinced me of the contrary. The gum-arabic, and some other similar substances, are the principal means of preparing the stone; and, by the acid, the stone is only rendered more disposed to admit it.2

This account seems to supersede the one given in the Specification of 18o1 in which Senefelder describes how the action of the acid raises the image on the surface of the stone ‘to the thickness of thin writing paper'. The application of acid is bound to remove a certain amount of the surface of the stone, but by emphasizing this part of the process Senefelder may easily have misled many early printers who saw in it something allied to the processes they already understood.' The establishment here of gum arabic as the ‘principal Ingredient for preparing the Stone'² must be considered important for the subsequent development of lithography.

In this chapter Senefelder also considers some of the difficulties which frequently confront the beginner owing to faulty preparation of the stone, and describes methods of surmounting them. The various instruments and equipment used in lithography, the steel pen, brush, etching needles, rollers, and inking balls are discussed in chapter four, and the papers and the damping of them in chapter five. The following chapter is devoted to lithographic presses, and Senefelder includes descriptions and illustrations of the basic types in use.³

The second practical section is concerned with the different manners of lithography and their application. It is in these chapters that Senefelder displays the wide range of lithography, or rather of the lithographic stone, for many of the methods he describes belong more properly to other branches of printing. All together he describes twenty different ‘manners', some so complicated that they were soon abandoned, others significant in that they anticipated later developments. These various manners can be classified under one or other of the two principal branches of lithography which Senefelder calls the elevated manner'(erhobene Manier) and the ‘engraved manner' (vertiefte Manier), or they can combine features of both.

In the first, all those parts of the stone that are covered by a greasy ink, resist the action of the acid poured over the whole surface of the stone, by means of which the other parts of the surface become corroded; they stand, therefore, higher than the latter, as elevated from the plane surface of the stone. In the second manner, all those lines or parts of the drawing or writing which are to give the impression, are engraved into the surface of the stone by means of a sharp needle, or bitten into it by the action of an acid.'

This is a confusing classification, for what we now call lithography, simple ink and chalk drawing or writing on stone, is included under Senefelder's ‘elevated manner'. It is hard to understand why he should have adopted this classification when he states quite clearly that the essential feature in the preparation of the stone is the application of gum arabic and not the acid. The latter only incidentally puts the image very slightly in relief, and Senefelder knew perfectly well that it was not the difference of levels that caused the stone to print in certain parts but not in others.² In fact, he was accepting a terminology for his new method of printing which stemmed directly from the older processes of wood-engraving and copper-engraving. The classification of lithography adopted by Marcel de Serres using the terms gravure au tracé and gravure entaillée is far more logical and less misleading.

Senefelder subdivides this so-called ‘elevated' branch of lithography into nine separate manners of working on the stone. The first of these to be described is straightforward drawing with prepared ink using either the pen or the hair brush (Plates 15, 16):

This is one of the principal manners of Lithography; and, perhaps, the most popular of all; as it is applicable to many purposes of common life. It not only imitates all sorts of writing and printing, and in many cases even surpasses them in elegance, but it is also applicable to all drawings where the nicety of a well executed copper-plate is not required.³

Senefelder explains that this manner had hitherto been used only for writing and for music, but he foresees its possibilities for the ‘higher productions of art', though he admits that up till now its progress had been hindered by the difficulty of using the pen on stone. The lines of the drawing should be bitten in'⁴ so that they become ‘elevated a little above the rest of the surface',5 but not too much or the spaces in between will begin to take colour. The strength of the etching must depend on the boldness of the design, but in any case the difference in relief that Senefelder recommends is very slight, as he suggests looking at the surface of the stone against the light to see whether it is sufficiently bitten.

5 Ibid.

3 Op. cit., p. 203.

The second manner, of chalk drawing on a grained stone resembling the surface of rough drawing paper, requires a much weaker etch. Senefelder sees this chalk manner as ‘a clear addition, and considerable acquisition to the art . . .',ı for the artist can draw directly on to the stone using a familiar instrument, and in far less time than it would take him on copper. The colour of the lithographic stone is a warmish grey and, in order to compensate for this tone when the image is printed on white paper, Senefelder describes a method of combining a drawing in the chalk manner with a tint stone of the same colour ahd, as a development of this, leaving blank spaces in the tint area to give the appearance of a chalk drawing heightened with white (Plate 17). He notes that this method of printing drawings with tint plates had already become greatly admired by amateurs, though he could hardly have anticipated the wide popularity of this type of lithograph some twenty years later. Senefelder also describes a method of inking up a chalk drawing in different colours by masking out certain areas with stencils, and suggests that colour prints could be even more effectively produced by using a separate stone for each colour.

The third of the elevated' manners Senefelder describes is the transfer method, by which a drawing or other image made in ink or chalk on specially prepared paper is transferred to lithographic stone and printed in the same way as a similar drawing made directly on the stone (Plates 19, 20).‘This manner is peculiar to the chemical printing,' writes Senefelder, ‘and I am strongly inclined to believe, that it is the principal and most important part of my discovery.'2 Its real significance was to do away with the need for reversing an image, and this was of great value both to artists, who could by this means obtain fac similes of their drawings',3 as well as to those concerned with writing, music printing, and other branches of commerce.

Manners four and five are essentially imitations of other processes. The first of these to be described is a method of producing the appearance of a woodcut with a facility which must make any wood-engraver envious (Plate 18). The dark parts of the design are completely covered with chemical ink, and when this coating is dry the lights are removed with an etching needle. Where the design is mainly one of black lines on a light ground the marks are put in with the pen. As his fifth manner Senefelder describes ‘Two sorts of India-Ink Drawing'. The initial procedure is very similar to that of the previous manner, but graded tones are obtained either by removing parts of the ground of chemical ink with mezzotint tools or by rubbing it away with a piece of cloth. This last technique required very careful assessment of the consistency of the ink and the strength of the acid. Senefelder was obviously excited by its possibilities, and was anticipating developments in France in the forties when he claimed that it was capable of surpassing even chalk lithography.

The sprinkled manner is another means of obtaining tones on the stone, and involves passing a knife along a tooth-brush charged with chemical ink and allowing the spatter to fall on the stone. Different densities of tone can be obtained by masking out the tints successively with sheets of paper and, later, by working them up by hand (Plates 21, 22). Finally, under this ‘elevated' manner, Senefelder includes various methods of printing in colour from separate plates, and printing in gold and silver.

The ‘engraved' manners are all imitative of the various processes applied to copper, and Senefelder describes seven of them. The most important of these, and Senefelder suggests one of the most important in the whole of lithography, especially for writing and for maps, is the ‘incised' manner (Plates 239). In this process a hard smooth stone is first of all covered with a coating of gum arabic coloured with lampblack or red chalk. When this is dry the drawing is traced on to the stone and the coating is incised with an ordinary etching needle. Where a light mark is required only as much of the coating is removed as is necessary to reveal the stone beneath, but for darker tones the needle is drawn repeatedly along the same line to produce an incision in the stone. As in copper-plate printing the tone of a line is proportionate to its depth, and the stone is charged in the same way as the metal by rubbing ink into the incised lines with a rag. The only real difference between these two kinds of engraving is that the stone is damped before being charged with ink. Ideally this should be sufficient to repel the ink from the surface of the stone, and it should need no wiping. More usually spots appear or a light tint begins to form, and the stone needs some cleaning with a damp rag. This manner, Senefelder explains, ‘is nearly equal to the best copperplate-printing . . . while the stone can be wrought rather more expeditiously and easily than copper . . .'.1

It was clearly Senefelder's intention to show that lithography could surpass all other processes in terms of facility and economy, but it was not so easy to do so as far as quality was concerned, since copper-engraving was held in such high esteem. In addition to this equivalent of stroke-engraving he describes lithographic methods of imitating etchings, soft-ground etchings, and even aquatints. His method of etching on stone is very similar to the same process on copper, the surface being covered with an etching ground which is then scratched away with a needle. But the stone had, according to Senefelder, an added advantage over copper in that the etching needle could be used with greater pressure to cut into the stone itself and so give considerably stronger lines in the subsequent etching. The other manners he mentions are too complicated to bé described in this account; and in general the engraved' branch of the process, with the exception of the ‘incised' and etched' manners, appears to have been little used.

Senefelder ends this account of the various manners of lithography by describing four additional methods of working on stone, which are various combinations of those already mentioned. He calls them mixed manners. Lithography, he boasts, possesses a peculiarity shared by no other medium, for engraved and raised lines can be printed from the same stone.

The Supplement, section three of the second part of the treatise, is strangely prophetic and gives some insight into the character of the ingenious inventor. In this he mentions some of the improvements which had been occupying his attention for some time, and which no doubt had been partly responsible for holding up the appearance of his treatise. He describes a method of printing by the chemical and mechanical manners at the same time and on different levels of the stone, which anticipates the work of printmakers in this century, and a method of imitating oil paintings by transferring separate colour prints successively from paper on to canvas. But most important of all is the account of his search for a substitute for the lithographic stone which was bulky and very costly to transport. In this final section he describes a method of producing artificial stone plates by coating paper and other substances with a preparation of clay, chalk, linseed oil, and other metallic oxides, and mentions some of his researches into the application of different metals to chemical printing. He explains that these had been somewhat delayed by the urgency of publishing his treatise, and that he hopes to deal with them more fully in a supplementary work. A collection of twelve drawings on planches lithographiques' eventually appeared in 1823,' but Senefelder could hardly have anticipated that within a hundred years the lithographic stone would have become more or less obsolete.

Mention must be made here of another prophetic passage, though it does not appear in this section, but in the chapter relating to presses. In this Senefelder admits that he considers it one of the greatest imperfections of lithography that the quality of printing depends to a large extent on the skill of the printer, and that ‘Till the voluntary action of the human hand is no longer necessary, and till the impression can be produced wholly by good machinery' he will not believe that the art of Lithography has approached its highest perfection.'

To a public that did not even understand the elementary prìnciples of lithography, and asked only for an explanation of these, such peeps into the future must have seemed both incredible and irrelevant. Readers must have been confused by the variety of manners which, as the writer has found to his cost in trying to prepare this synopsis, are exceedingly difficult to understand even with some practical knowledge of working on stone. The accounts themselves are clearly enough written, and every step in the practical application of each manner is described minutely; but the very scope of the work precludes it from being very useful to the prospective lithographic printer. This wide range is seen as a point in its favour by Ackermann, who writes in the advertisement to the English edition:

Nothing seems to have escaped the notice of the ingenious author; his directions extend to every particular which has the least connexion with the subject . . .²

It is much more likely, however, that such thoroughness would have been regarded as a positive disadvantage by most beginners trying to understand the process, and this view is supported by at least two early lithographic printers, Houbloup³ and Engelmann.4

Lithography was at the time in a state of bewildering complexity, and this is reflected in Senefelder's treatise. If there was one person who could have given the process direction, it was the inventor himself; and yet he seemed reluctant even to commit himself to the extent of selecting a limited number of manners and, thereby, presenting lithography in a simpler form. Though he was prophetic in predicting some of the later technical developments, he was not as helpful as either Marcel de Serres or Mairet in pointing out what was likely to be the most important branch of lithography in the immediate future. Throughout the treatise no emphasis is put on the elevated' manner (which we would call planographic), and the ‘engraved' manner is considered to be equally important:

In general, therefore, it is difficult, if not impossible, to say which of these two manners is the most advantageous, as this will depend, in a great degree, on the subject, and the feeling of the artist.

While Senefelder allows that the ‘elevated' manner will give longer runs and speedier printing, he admits that the ‘engraved' manner will give a greater nicety of expression. Each occupies much the same space in the text. If we are to look for guidance also from the twenty plates chosen as specimens for the original German edition it would seem that Senefelder thought them equally important, for six are in the engraved manner, six are done with the pen, one with chalk, and the rest are either transfers or examples of mixed methods.² Praise is lavished equally on both branches, but if we are to single out one manner which receives an abnormal amount it must be the process of transferring an image from paper to stone. Apart from this, we must conclude that Senefelder found it difficult to choose between one branch of his invention which was quite different from all known processes and would naturally be greeted with a certain amount of suspicion, and the other branch which might be more readily accepted simply because it was an extension of existing methods and yet offered the possibility of improving upon them.

It seems that Senefelder was quite incapable of sensing which way lithography was moving, and that after nearly twenty years he was further from expressing the essence of his invention than he was when writing his patent specification. By concentrating attention almost equally on what he called the ‘engraved' and the ‘elevated' manners Senefelder failed in his treatise to give a clear and lucid description of lithography and, consequently, failed to give any positive direction to its course over the next few years.

# 1819-1825: THE POPULARIZATION OF LITHOGRAPHY AND THE GROWTH OF A TECHNIQUE

LHAT SENEFELDER'S HANDBOOK WAS NOT THE SOLUTION TO all the problems of the prospective lithographic draughtsman and printer is borne out by the number of treatises which appeared in the years immediately following its publication; though it is probably equally true that some of these could not have been produced without the help of the inventor's book as a guide and source of reference. At least a dozen new lithographic treatises were published between 1819 and 1825, and some of them also appeared either in translation or in a second edition within this same period. If such versions are included, then nearly twenty editions of these post-Senefelder treatises appeared in the space of only seven years, most of them being published in Germany, France, and England.'

This is the period of the most important lithographic treatises as far as the direction of the future course of lithography is concerned, and amongst their authors we find the two leading printers of the second quarter of the nineteenth century—Engelmann in France and Hullmandel in England. Until this time writers on lithography had been principally concerned with describing the variety of processes that could be applied to the stone, and most manuals were addressed to the potential lithographic printer. In this later group of treatises lithography is greatly simplified, and the planographic method of straightforward chalk and ink drawing is firmly established as the most important, if not the only, branch of lithography for

the pictorial artist. Considerably more emphasis is given to instructing artists how to draw on the stone, and these treatises provide our best source of information on the techniques of the early lithographic draughtsmen.

In the year that Senefelder's book appeared in France and England, Raucourt de Charleville published a manual which was to have considerable influence: Ménoire sur les expériences lithographiques faites à l'Ecole Royale des Ponts et Chaussées de France; ou manuel théorique et pratique du dessinateur et de l'imprimeur lithographes (Toulon, 1819). In the preface Raucourt explains that when he was asked to set up a press at the above establishment he was completely in the dark, even though the process was becoming more popular. German printers were reluctant to divulge the secret to foreigners, and Engelmann and Lasteyrie, the two French printers who knew the process well, kept it a secret through jealousy of one another. Eventually he received from a German some notes containing information about the process ‘que j’avais vainement sollicités de mes compatriotes .. .'1 and learnt the rest by trial and error.

Raucourt mentions the articles by Marcel de Serres in the Annales des arts et manufactures, but he seems to have been unaware of the works of Mairet, D\*\*\*, L. J. D. B., or Senefelder, for he continues in the preface:

. . . j’espérais que des hommes connus du public se décideraient à l’instruire; mais depuis deux ans, il me semble démontré que personne n'y songe, bien que le nombre des Lithographes s’augmente: tous ceux que j’ai connus sont maîtrisés par leurs affaires, leurs obligations, ou leurs intérêts.

Par un concours de circonstances qui me sont favorables, je me trouve peut-être le seul entre les hommes qui se sont sérieusement occupés de la Lithographic, qui n’aie point de motifs d’en faire un mystère; . . . je ne serai d’ailleurs ingrat envers personne, puisque personne ne m'a dit son secret, et mes obligations seraient nulles, si le mémoire de M. Marcel de Serres ne m’avait servi de guide.

Je trouve, au contraire, de puissans motifs de publier des observations qui m’appartiennent, dans le silence dcs personnes qui depuis long-tems auraient pu publier les leurs.²

It seems that in France at least the veil of secrecy was not lifted with the publication of Senefelder's work, as Peignot too makes no mention of it in his bibliography. In England the position may well have been somewhat different for, in the translator's preface to the English edition of Raucourt's treatise published a year later, Hullmandel refers to the existing literature on lithography and seems to be hinting at Senefelder's Complete course of lithography:

The books which have hitherto been published on lithography are very imperfect, and are much more adapted to persons who already understand the art than to those who wish to learn it: they explain, it is true, the different styles to which lithography may be applied, and the preparation of the chalks, inks, and stones, but give no clue to the labyrinth in which a beginner is soon bewildered, when he once attempts the practical part of printing from stone.1

Raucourt's treatise was probably the first to fulfil this need, and at the same time become well known. Hullmandel describes it in his preface to the English edition as ‘the best work which has ever been published on this art'.² In 1839 Brégeaut found it ‘encore fort utile, malgré les progrès faits dans cet art, depuis l’époque de sa publication',3 and even as late as 1842 Desportes claimed that it was still le seul ouvrage sur la lithographie qui soit écrit avec clarté, avec méthode et surtout avec conscience'.4 Some measure of its success can be gathered from the fact that Hullmandel's translation appeared within a year and ran into three editions.⁵ Its influence too on the translator, who had only recently begun his experiments in lithography, should not be underestimated.

All editions arc octavo, and in terms of length stand midway between the rather brief early manuals and Senefelder's lengthy work. The treatise is divided into four parts. In the first of these Raucourt describes the basic principles of lithography and the various materials used. For some time these could not be procured readily from manufacturers, and the early lithographic printer was usually obliged to make his own materials. Like most later writers Raucourt describes clearly how the various chalks and drawing and printing inks are manufactured. In many cases the lithographer would have to construct his own presses as well, or be prepared to have them brought from some distance.6 The two basic types of presses and other utensils like tables, rollers, moulds for making chalk and tools for cutting them square are discussed in part two. In the third part Raucourt describes clearly, and in a very practical way, the precautions to be taken when drawing on stone. This is the part many contemporaries must have found most useful, for he does not follow the usual division of lithography into manners, but concentrates on the planographic branch of chalk and pen drawing. Only very brief mention is made of imitating woodcuts and etchings on stone, and such images are to be printed planographically.' He supplies careful instructions for the artist, and warns him to take special care not to allow his breath or any saliva to fall on the stone, nor to allow his fingers to touch it as they will leave greasy marks. These and similar observations are amplified in Hullmandel's own treatise, and will be considered in more detail below. They do not appear for the first time with Raucourt's manual, but such recommendations are repeated and grow in complexity from one account to another. Similar useful instructions are given for the preparation and printing of stones, and the section relating to printing inks Hullmandel found to be the most important part of the treatise.

The last section of the book is mainly devoted to a most revealing comparison of lithography and copper-plate printing, together with a detailed study of their relative costs. The principal advantage of lithography over copper-engraving was its economy, and while Raucourt admits that a lithographic drawing cannot compete with the very best engravings on copper he feels that it does have advantages over the laboured, run-of-the-mill engraving:

On peut affirmer que la Lithographie remplacera cette partie, la plus usuelle, la plus étendue de la gravure; beaucoup de ses produits ont déjà autant de perfection et de plus ils sont authographes, on en a des exemples dans les belles compositions historiques et les paysages lithographiés en France, dans lesquels on retrouve la touche franche et spirituelle des grands maîtres qui les ont faits; c’est alors qu'on voit la Lithographie prendre ses avantages et l’emporter sur la gravure qui porte toujours l’empreinte des peines qu’elle a données au graveur.²

He then gives in tabulated form a comparison of the costs of the two processes.3 This shows a slight advantage in the use of stone (assuming it to be used thirty times) over copper, but the greatest economy lay in the actual making of the image. Raucourt estimates the lithographic draughtsman's fee to be less than a quarter of that of the engraver, and this, together with the saving in the cost of materials, meant that the drawing could be made on stone for a seventh of the cost of an engraving. Printing costs only slightly favoured lithography so that the economy was proportionally most noticeable on short runs; but the initial difference in costs remained an important factor however many were printed. Raucourt estimates that a copper-plate would stand an edition of only 2,4oo whereas the stone could give any number of impressions.1

Like Mairet before him, Raucourt de Charleville is important because he simplified the process by selecting planographic printing from simple chalk or ink drawings as the essential part of lithography. The same may also be said of Houbloup who deals only with this branch of lithography in his Théorie lithographique of 1825, and says of engraving on stone that ‘Malgré les soins que quelques dessinateurs ont donnés au perfectionnement de ce genre, la gravure sur pierre est presque entièrement abandonnée . . .'.² With the publication of these treatises, and those of Engelmann and Hullmandel, lithography is quite clearly established as far as the artist is concerned as a planographic process, though occasional references to the engraved methods are to be found well into the second half of the nineteenth century as a sort of additional advantage possessed by lithography.

So far, those printers who had written treatises on lithography had not been, with the exception of Senefelder, important lithographic printers themselves. But within two years of one another, in 1822 and 1824 respectively, the leading lithographic printers of the times published treatises— Engelmann in France and Hullmandel in England. Both were primarily concerned with producing prints from drawings made by artists and had already come into contact with leading lithographic draughtsmen of the period, and their treatises are addressed specifically to those intending to draw on stone. Earlier treatises describe in full all the equipment used in lithography and the whole process from the choosing of the stone to the final printing, and were intended, presumably, for those who wanted to set up a press of their own. In these two handbooks only as much as the artist would need to know for the actual drawing on stone is described. By the time they produced their treatises both Engelmann and Hullmandel had established themselves as successful lithographic printers and were in competition with many others. It is easy to understand why they may have chosen to confine the limits of their subject, especially when, by addressing the artist specifically, they were also educating a possible clientele for their own presses. Raucourt de Charleville's treatise can be considered transitional in that it dealt somewhat more fully than others with this part of the process, but those of Engelmann and Hullmandel are the first to confine themselves wholly to the art of drawing on stone.'

The first of these treatises to appear was Engelmann's Manuel du dessinateur lithographe (Paris, 1822) (Plates 32, 33). Engelmann's aim in writing the book was, he explains in the introduction, to describe les moyens les plus faciles, les plus sûrs et les plus prompts d’exécuter des Dessins sur pierre . . .'.2 But he takes particular pride in bringing to notice his own discovery of lavis' or ‘aqua tinta lithographique', which we cannot but feel is partly the reason for publication. This is a method of producing the appearance of wash drawings on stone, and pages 4-16 are devoted to reprinting an extract from the Bulletin de la Société d’Encouragement pour l'Industrie Nationale in which M. Mérimée, in an address given to that Society in 1821, vouches for having been witness to the first results obtained by this process in June 1819. This is followed (pages 18–39) by a report made by M. Castellan to the Académie des Beaux-Arts in 1816, Rapport sur la lithographie et particulièrement sur un recueil de dessins lithographiés par M. Engelmann. These two topics occupy nearly half the treatise which, in the circumstances, must be seen primarily as a piece of self-justification and as an advertisement for his press.

Hullmandel's The art of drawing on stone (London, 1824) (Plates 34, 35) was the most important English treatise on lithography to be published in the first half of the nineteenth century. It was one of the few early treatises to appear in a large format (imperial octavo) and was, with the exception of the English edition of Senefelder's work, the most expensively produced. It is a somewhat larger book than Engelmann's and as the whole of the text, apart from the introduction, is limited to the subject of the title it is far more complete in its instructions. There is, however, a distinct similarity between the two treatises both in the text and in the choice of specimen plates; and Hullmandel's debt to Engelmann is clearly acknowledged in his account of the ‘dabbing style', which is his term for the process of lavis lithographique. Though they cover much the same ground Hullmandel is much more orderly than Engelmann. He divides lithography into three basic styles—chalk drawing', the dabbing style', and ‘pen and ink drawing'—and does not even mention the engraved method. Engelmann makes no such definite divisions, though it is clear that these three methods are also considered by him to be the most important. He does mention the engraved method, but only to say that it is no longer employed except by those with an incomplete knowledge of pen drawing.

The methods described in the two treatises are more or less the same, and the similarities must be considered the result of direct influence rather than just the reflection of common practice.' Hullmandel is more detailed in his instructions than Engelmann, although the germ of most of his observations can be found in the earlier work. The two printers themselves exerted a powerful influence on the course of lithography, both through their own printing establishments, and through the publication of their treatises. Engelmann's book appeared in a second edition within two years, and a third was published in 1829; Hullmandel's also ran into two subsequent editions, 1833 and 1835.² Between them these two lithographers established a method of drawing on stone which remained virtually unchanged for two decades, and their treatises will be used here as a basis for an account of the various ways in which an artist would normally draw on stone during this period.

The stone would first be prepared for the draughtsman by the lithographic printer, or more usually by one of his assistants. Though calling for some judgement and care this was largely a menial task and was usually given to an apprentice on entering the workshop. The stone was given a grain or polished according to whether a chalk or ink drawing was intended, but this part of the process is discussed in those treatises which are more concerned with the work of the printer. It is not described by either Engelmann or Hullmandel as the work required considerable manual labour and would not normally have been done by the artist himself.' Stones already prepared for drawing were often advertised by printers for hire, together with the various materials needed for drawing on them.² Hullmandel used to prepare stones with a special facing, and devotes a section to the packing of them in wooden cases for ‘Persons residing in the country, or in distant towns ...'3 There are also prints in existence which show stones being carried to clients (Plate 36),4 but owing to the weight of the limestone slabs we must assume that, if it was convenient to do so, it was more usual to work at the printer's. This was certainly so by the middle of the nineteenth century when printers usually put aside a special room where their trained lithographic draughtsmen could draw on stone.

This part of the proceedings was not the simple process it is now, but the artist sat at a special desk (Plate 37) complete with a number of ingenious devices for making his job easier. The stone was placed on a turntable, which allowed its great weight to be turned at ease, and supports at either side held a wooden bridge which prevented the hands from touching the surface of the stone. At the back of the desk was a shelf to take the original drawing, and behind this was a mirror, fixed to a hinged flap, which reversed the image for the artist. Such complicated pieces of furniture continued to be used throughout the ninteenth century; in 1827 C. F. Müller, a painter, took out a patent in Paris for a lithographer's desk with a turntable which could be tilted' and special desks werc also illustrated by Lemercier² and Doyen³ more than fifty years later. Hullmandel recommends only the use of a wooden bridge to prevent the hands from soiling the stone (Plate 38). The artist is warned by both writers to take infinite care when working, he must not breathe on the stone, must take care to turn his head away when he sneezes, and must not allow any scurf or saliva to fall on the drawing. Later writers recommend the wearing of a cap⁴ and mask⁵ to prevent such disasters, and Richmond even suggests that the head, face, and beard should be washed before commencing lithography.6 Such precautions have since been felt unnecessary, partly because fashions have changed, and partly because our attitude to the process is now quite different; but it is important to realize that for the type of lithograph produced at this time, depending on exquisite craftsmanship, such accidents could prove disastrous to a drawing. Both Engelmann and Hullmandel provide illustrations of what would happen to a stone should any of the above accidents occur (Plates 41, 42). Fingerprints would begin to pick up grease from the roller, scurf would appear as a pattern of black dots, and where saliva falls the drawing ink or chalk would be prevented fron reaching the stone causing a series of white marks to appear. These matters of lithographic hygiene are mentioned in some detail only to show how standards of craftsmanship in lithography have changed, and to emphasize that in the early nineteenth century lithography was not as spontaneous a medium as has often been maintained by modern writers.

Before beginning a drawing on stone the artist must first clear away all dust with a clean brush which is kept specially for this purpose, he must never under any circumstances try to blow it away for reasons already mentioned. When this is done the artist is free to begin the tracing on stone. The drawing can be outlined by using a lead pencil directly on to the stone, though if it is used too heavily it may prevent the grease from penetrating the surface. Some writers suggest placing the pencil drawing face down on the stone and pulling it through the press, but Hullmandel and Engelmann both agree that the best method is to use red chalk, either on tracing paper (Hullmandel), or by coating a piece of thin paper with it and slipping this between the tracing and the stone (Engelmann). A margin of at least half an inch must be left, according to Hullmandel, between the extremities of the drawing and the edges of the stone.

An ink drawing requires a polished stone and may be done with either a steel pen or a sable brush. Hullmandel prefers the former, while Engelmann contends that a sable brush with all but a few hairs cut away is easier to handle as the pen tends to blot. The special ink is made by dissolving sticks of chemical ink in distilled water, but it will not keep long in a liquid state (Hullmandel puts the limit at three days). It is important to get the right consistency; if the ink is too thick it will tend to cling to the pen, while if not thick enough it may not print successfully. The ink applied with the hair brush will need to flow more freely than that used with the pen. Both writers are emphatic in pointing out that the ink must be used sufficiently dense to print black. They emphasize that it is not the black pigment in the ink which causes the drawing to print but its greasy content, and that this either is, or is not, sufficient to affect the surface of the stone. The effects of brush strokes and washes of ink when applied to the stone in graded strengths are therefore completely unpredictable. They may print black or they may not print at all. Both Hullmandel and Engelmann supply plates showing the failure of such over-diluted washes of lithographic ink (Plates 40, 41).

This was clearly a real limitation to the draughtsman who was used to applying ink or water-colour of any consistency to paper, and could achieve a wide range of tone within the individual marks made by a pen or brush. Turning to lithography, he was more or less forced to accept a method of drawing rather similar to that used by the engravers, who achieve an optical effect of tones by the use of lines. Hullmandel insists that ‘Whatever effect is intended in an ink drawing, it must be done by thicker or thinner lines,crossed in various directions, as in a copper-plate etching, the ink being always used equally thick',' while Engelmann contends that whether the pen or brush is employed drawings must be done either ‘par hachures ou au pointillé'.' These are methods of drawing which might well have been followed in any case by artists who were brought up in the tradition of copper-engraving. There are strong traces of copper-engraving techniques even in contemporary wood-engraving where they are extremely difficult to produce.² While lithography was setting itself up as a rival process to copper-engraving it was only natural that it should borrow some of its techniques from the established medium, but this influence probably lasted longer than it might otherwise have done owing to the technical limitations of the new process. When it was first invented lithography was hailed as a method of multiplying the pen or chalk drawing of any amateur. This was later found to be true only with certain reservations: the drawing would be multiplied only if it was the right sort of drawing in the first place. Engelmann suggests that those wanting to draw with pen on stone would do best to spend some time in his workshops, and Hullmandel points out that the artist should always say to himself Is what I have just done, drawn so as to print well?'3

Though more difficult than the chalk as far as drawing was concerned, ink lithographs were much easier to print and they gave longer runs. Engelmann held that such a drawing was practically inexhaustible, and that thirty to forty thousand prints had been taken without any appreciable deterioration.
