Lithography now assumed the feature of engraving, and the powers of this new art showing itself in highly-finished drawings, artists of reputation gradually abandoned it, and it got entirely into the hands of lithographic draughtsmen.'

Hullmandel hoped that his lithotint would restore to lithography its most important feature by ‘giving the original works of great masters’² in all their richness and variety of tone. The writer of the article in L’Artiste makes the same claim for Lemercier's lavis sur pierre and points out that from now on painters could ‘tracer eux-mêmes sur la pierre toutes les fantaisies de leur imagination . . .'3 without resorting to the tedious business of building up their tones by chalk hatching which required considerable training and experience.

Any of these tonal processes, Lemercier's two versions of lavis lithographique, Hullmandel's stump style and lithotint, d'Orschwiller's process, Tudot's manière noire, or any combination of them, could also be applied to the tint stone which, from the mid thirties onwards, began to be used more and more frequently to support a black drawing.4 The ink used for the printing of these tints had to be mixed by the printers themselves and, because its consistency was not so satisfactory as that of black ink, such tint stones were usually less well printed. In most cases, therefore, it is even more difficult than with black lithographs to decide exactly how their tones were produced.

We have seen that the tint stones provided as examples in the treatises of Engelmann and Hullmandel were only of a very rudimentary nature. Hullmandel's contained some slight effects of half-tone produced by scratching parallel lines with an etching needle, while Engelmann's contained none at all. The process was seen rather as a survival from an earlier period, for by the time they came to write their treatises both printers had virtually abandoned the technique.' Like Ackermann, Engelmann and Hullmandel had been inspired to experiment in this style after having seen the tinted lithographs made in Munich by Strixner and Piloty for the Oeuvres lithographiques, and some of the first lithographs each of these three produced were in the tinted style.² Other printers, too, produced a few early lithographs with tint stones added. The process was never entirely forgotten but, except in Germany where it continued to be'used for the reproduction of paintings, it gradually fell into disuse after about 182o.

It was revived and considerably improved by Hullmandel around the year 1835, and over the next two decades tint plates show an increasing range of tone owing to the use of many of the processes described above.³ Hullmandel's scrap-book contains a number of experiments in producing graded tints in skies which appear to have been drawn by a mixture of these methods, and one of them bears the note ‘Trial for producing variety of tint in a tint stone, which enabled Mr. Harding to produce his sketches at Home & abroad & established and introduced what has since been called, the tinted style in 1835’ (Plates 76, 77).4 In The art of drawing on stone Hullmandel had quite clearly stated that half-tones could not be produced by a partial scraping away of the waxy preparation. Improvements in lithography had now made this possible with the result that the tint stone ceased to be merely a support to an image, but became on occasions so much a part of it that the black stone would have appeared meaningless if printed on its own.

Except for a few passing references the treatises published in the period 1825-35 do not describe the tinted style at all; but it was taken up again in the works of Brégeaut (1839), Chevallier and Langlumé (1838), Engelmann (1835-40), and Fielding (1842) and by 1840 was once again one of the accepted techniques of lithography. In England the process was usually called ‘the tinted style'while in France it was known as printing à deux teintes or the prints as épreuves rehaussées. The method of working remained much the same as that described by Engelmann and Hullmandel some fifteen years earlier, but the range of tone was extended by the application of any number of the negative processes mentioned above. Engelmann describes the English practice as follows:

Dans la nouvelle application que les anglais ont faite de ces planches teintées, ils ne se sont pas contentés de rehausser leurs dessins par des lumières vives et coupées net; ils les ont en même temps dégradées et amenées par de douces transitions, du ton de la teinte jusqu'au blanc. On fait un grand mystère encore des moyens employés pour obtenir cet effet, et les dessinateurs s’efforcent, en frottant les pierres avec des chiffons, de produire des demi-teintes; ou bien ils essaient de les enlever au grattoir, en employant pour cela des pierres grenées.1

Fielding recommends either stopping out the pure whites with gum or scraping them away with a knife and, for those parts intended as half-tints, merely partial scraping away of the greasy preparation. He also suggests using a piece of woollen cloth for rubbing down the defined edges left by the gum and for producing softer effects like the gradations of evening skies.²

Engelmann, who did not live to see the full flowering of the tinted lithograph, describes two alternative methods in his later treatise.3 One of these was an ingenious method based on d'Orschwiller's idea for removing an image from the stone by drawing on it through a piece of paper. Engelmann suggests taking a counterproof of the black drawing on a piece of tinted paper and placing it face upwards on to a coarsely grained stone which has previously been covered with a ground of varnish. The artist then draws in the lights with a hard white crayon so that the varnish is removed from the stone in proportion to the pressure applied.4 This method had the great advantage that the artist could assess the work accurately as he progressed and, according to Engelmann, the marks produced in the final print looked just like those of a crayon drawing itself. Absolute whites could be obtained by scraping the stone in the usual way when the paper had been removed. Engelmann's other method was a positive one in which the tint was built up with the lithographic chalk as in a normal black drawing, but much more strongly because it was to be printed in a pale tone. When this halftone structure was complete full tones could be put in with ink or, alternatively, the lights and half-tones could be stopped out and a roller charged with printing ink passed over the whole stone.

From these descriptions it is evident that there was nothing particularly new in the process as such; the novelty merely lay in the application of methods which had previously been used for black lithographs to the production of supporting tint stones. The approach was normally a' negative one simply because it originated from the early function of the tint stone, which was to serve as a solid background to counteract the whiteness of the printing paper. All the same, the negative approach had one great advantage over straightforward chalk and ink drawing on stone. If the highlights were scraped so deeply that the stone became substantially hollow, the paper would be pushed into the stone during the printing and so become embossed in the final print (Plates 78, 79). Such an effect may have arisen quite accidentally in the first place,i but it was certainly produced intentionally later on, and Engelmann describes a similar method in which a very strong etch is used to make the lights appear in relief‘comme si elles avaient été posées avec du blanc au pinceau'.² This embossing of highlights in a tint stone was used to great effect in England by Hullmandel and Harding, and to a lesser extent by Cheffins, Day & Haghe, and Michael Hanhart.

Tint stones were nearly always printed in a rather warm buff colour, and there were probably a number of reasons for this conservatism. In his treatise Senefelder had recommended the use of a printed tint to give the paper something like the tone and colour of the lithographic stone on which the drawing was originally made. When India paper began to be used for the printing of lithographs in 1820³ it soon took the place of the tint stone as its colour was also very similar to that of the lithographic stone. India paper also happened to be an ideal printing surface for delicate chalk drawings, but its supplementary purpose of providing a slight tonal background is borne out by prints on it with highlights touched in by hand with opaque white.' In its turn the tint stone was used to imitate the effect of printing on India paper, and Chevallier and Langlumé describe the appearance of a tint stone as being ‘analogue à celle qu'offrent les dessins tirés sur papier de Chine',2 and devote a section to the imitation of India paper by means of tint stones.3 There were probably antiquarian reasons as well for the popularity of these buff tints which were often very much warmcr than the colour of any lithographic stone or sheet of India paper. The chiaroscuro woodcuts of the sixteenth century almost certainly accounted for the popularity of the tinted style in Germany, and the familiar custom of colouring monochrome aquatints with sepia and similar coloured washes, in the manner of Gilpin's illustrations to his own picturesque tours, may have been partly responsible for the success of the tinted lithograph in England. When particular effects like sunsets or night scenes were required other colours were occasionally used for the tint stones, and when printing with two tints became popular in the forties one of them was usually printed in a grey-blue colour for the sky.

Such tints were normally printed after the black working, and with a transparent ink. Engelmann notes that printers in Munich used to put white chalk in their inks to make them roll up more evenly,4 but he does not recommend this practice because the ink becomes opaque as the oil in it dries, with the result that the black drawing underneath loses its richness. The alternative was to print the tint first, but this had one great disadvantage over the normal practice because the subsequent black impression flattened out any relief obtained in the highlights by scraping and strong etching. The registration of tint stones was normally by means of pin-holes in opposite corners of the print, and special wooden compasses were devised to lay the paper on the stone.⁵

The tinted style of lithography was widely used from the mid thirties onwards for about a quarter of a century. Its revival was largely the result of Hullmandel's example, but it soon spread to most of Europe and became almost the accepted idiom for lithography by the middle of the century.⁶

The style became particularly popular in England and the finest examples, at least from the technical standpoint, were certainly produced in this country. The tinted lithographs of Harding and other artists which Hullmandel printed were greatly admired in France, and this was one of the few branches of lithography in which even French writers admitted that English artists and printers excelled.

By 184o all the main techniques used by artists for drawing on stone had really been evolved. Improvements now rested largely with the printer and were directed principally at finding a satisfactory method of producing colour prints by lithography. There had been numerous isolated examples of printing by lithography with coloured inks dating right back to the beginning of the century, and some of these involved the use of several stones, but no comprehensive system was developed until the late thirties.² In December 1836, however, Engelmann père et fils perfected a method of colour printing and presented some examples of it to the Société industrielle de Mulhouse. Their process was similar in principle to the one adopted by Le Blon for intaglio printing early in the eighteenth century. Like Le Blon, Engelmann used the three primary colours, red, blue, and yellow, each of which was printed separately. The drawing was done in the normal way with either pen or chalk and the final colour of the print depended on the relative solidity of the tints drawn on the stones. Where necessary Engelmann used a fourth black printing, as Le Blon's successors had done, to give added richness of tone. On 31 July 1837 Engelmann and his son were granted a patent for ten years for their new process,3 which they were later to call chromolithographie. Even before the death of Engelmann senior in 1839 a number of artists had tried the process, and an Albuin chromo-lithographique was published about 1838 consisting of eight coloured lithographs.⁴

Soon after Engelmann announced his discovery, Hullmandel developed an alternative approach to colour printing which was really an extension of tinted lithography. It was used to great effect in one of the finest of all lithographed publications, Boys's Picturesque architecture in Paris, Ghent, Antwerp, Rouen (London, 1839), where the process is referred to as Chromalithography.1 There are also instances of lithographs produced in the early forties in England where the tinted style has been developed so far that it can really be called colour printing. This underlines the point that has already been made in connection with Engelmann's process, that the actual methods used for drawing on stone remained the same as they had been for monochrome and tinted lithography. The novelty of the processes of both Engelmann and Hullmandel lay in the division of the image into a number of separate printings, in the manufacture of the coloured inks, and especially in the exact registration of the impressions. These were problems which concerned the technician rather than the artist, and it is perhaps because of this that chromolithography was soon taken out of the hands of artists and for nearly fifty years became almost entirely a commercial process.

A new era began for lithography around the year 184o, largely because of the rapid development of chromolithography. From this date printers tended to become more interested in the commercial side of lithography, and this is reflected in the later treatises which are far more concerned with the industrial and economic aspects of the process. The change is really marked by two publications, both of them French. In 1837 the first trade journal of lithography began to appear, and in 184o the publication of Engelmann's second treatise was completed. No works of a similar scale were written on lithography during the same period in England, where letterpress printing was probably more firmly rooted than in France, but there is every reason to believe that the attitudes reflected in these two publications were common to both countries.

Le Lithographe² appeared at irregular, but mainly monthly, intervals from June 1837 to March 1848. It was published under the direction of the lithographic printer Jules Desportes, who had already written a treatise of his own. He seems to have written many of the articles himself and his firm printed most of the plates as well. Le Lithographe began by having something of the character of a magazine of the arts, and included a few articles of general interest, but these were entirely dropped in the second year. It was intended for both artists and printers but, though there were reviews of lithographs exhibited at the Salon and other places, the emphasis was more on the commercial aspects of the process. The kinds of topics that attracted attention were zincography, engraving on stone, transferring from old and new books, writing on stone, typolithography, chromolithography, the apprenticeship system, rates of pay for workmen, the laws relating to the press, and reviews of industrial exhibitions. Some of these subjects had of course been mentioned in earlier treatises, and Le Lithographe does include some articles which would have interested the pictorial artist, but the industrial and commercial aspects of lithography are given much more emphasis here than in any of the treatises. The commercial printing from stone of maps, circulars, and music had been practised alongside pictorial lithography right from the beginning of the century, but by 184o it was seen, at least in France, as a potential rival to letterpress printing. Le Lithographe published lists giving the names of over 1,ooo lithographic printers in France,1 over 3oo of them in Paris, and in a report to the Société d'Encouragement in June 1839 Lasteyrie claimed that there were in France 750 lithographic establishments, 3,000 presses, and 10,000 people engaged in either printing, working on stone, or making lithographic equipment.² Though some of these presses specialized in the printing of artists' drawings, the majority must have been engaged in ordinary jobbing printing—and it was for such printers that the numbers of Le Lithographe were intended.

Engelmann's Traité théorique et pratique de lithographie (Mulhouse, 1835-40) was by far the longest and most comprehensive treatise on lithography yet to have appeared, running into over 45o pages of a quarto volume. It was the first of the exhaustive works on lithography, and in its emphasis on the commercial side of the process anticipates the treatises of Richmond, Doyen, Lorilleux, and Lemercier in the last quarter of the nineteenth century. Engelmann saw his book as a definitive work and, with the exception of his own chromolithography, describes every known aspect of the process in great detail besides giving a thorough survey of the theory on which it rested and of the chemical properties of the materials used. The treatise is very much a record of the progress of lithography over the twenty or so years that Engelmann had been associated with the process. In addition to the practical parts, which have been referred to on occasions elsewhere, it includes a long historical introduction, a good bibliography, and lists of the awards made to lithographers at the Expositions des Produits de l’Industrie française and by the Société d'Encouragement. Engelmann himself rightly felt that this was the end of a chapter in the history of lithography and, in the section of his treatise where he refers to the development of chromolithography, he makes the following claim that time has fully justified:

La Lithographie en noir me semble arrivée aujourd'hui à un tel point de perfection, que je crois peu probable qu’elle puisse jamais livrer des épreuves sensiblement plus belles que celles qui sortent à présent de certains ateliers. Je ne doute pas qu'’on ne trouve par la suite des moyens de production plus réguliers et plus économiques; mais sous le point de vue artistique, la Lithographie en noir me paraît arrivée à peu près à son apogée. C'est donc, si j'’ose m'exprimer ainsi, un art fait, dont les procédés sont devenus susceptibles d’une discussion théorique.1

I Traité, p. 54.

III

# TOPOGRAPHICAL LITHOGRAPHY IN ENGLAND AND FRANCE

# INTRODUCTION

I HE TECHNIQUES OF DRAWING FOR LITHOGRAPHY DESCRIBED IN the early treatises found their fullest expression in topography and landscape. Indeed, most of the methods of drawing on stone discussed in the previous chapter appear to have been evolved largely with the specific requirements of this branch of work in mind. Chalk drawing remained the basic technique, but the development of the processes of lavis lithographique, the stump style, tinted lithography, and lithotint towards a tonal system for lithography are all best studied in topography. Moreover, the skills of lithographic draughtsmen and printers in the first half of the nineteenth century were nowhere more obviously displayed.

Most such topographical lithographs were published in sets, either in wrappers, in a portfolio, or bound in volume form. Their first appearance in both England and France can be fairly accurately dated to the end of the second decade of the nineteenth century, with production tailing off some forty years later. Some were published with text, others without any, thus avoiding the statutory presentation to copyright libraries; but in any case the plates usually have a physical independence of the letterpress, and were often the raison d’être for publication. The prints themselves cover a wide field and take the form of picturesque representations of British and European cities, landscape views at home and on the continent, and records of newly explored lands.

Lithography inherited the traditions of eighteenth-century publishing by subscription, in which expensive books with plates usually appeared in parts with paper wrappers and were bound by the owner to his own taste, or could be bought collectively in bound form at the close of publication. Alternative versions were often issued on large and small paper, and were coloured by hand or left plain according to the pocket of the subscriber. But all this was established practice before lithography ever came on the scene, and many such topographical books had been produced with copperengraved or aquatinted plates, particularly in England. Lithography began nothing new in this branch of publishing, but continued a tradition in competition with aquatint, producing at first expensive folio volumes of the editions de luxe class. Only later, when a newly educated poorer clientele emerged, did lithography really enter the field of cheaper publishing and the realm of illustration proper. In the first half of the nineteenth century lithographs were only rarely produced as single prints, and a better market for them certainly existed if some excuse could be found for bringing a number together to be sold in sets by book- and print-seller alike. Topographical publications provided an assured market and an ideal opportunity for lithography to establish itself, and proved to be the means by which it eventually became accepted as a graphic process worthy of the attention of professional draughtsmen.

It was in works of this kind that a method of drawing for lithography was evolved and, at the same time, put to the test; for what might work well experimentally in the lithographic studio, and be described enthusiastically in a treatise, may not necessarily stand up to the hazards of commercial lithography. Thus it was that while many of the seeds sown in the treatises bore fruit and flourished in these topographical works, others proved to be suspect and were either improved upon or rejected. The experience gained from producing lithographs for publication had an important influence on the techniques described in later treatises; for just as the professional copperengravers of the seventeenth century evolved a technique which would stand the wear of a long edition, so the lithographic draughtsman, though not so obviously, moulded his method of drawing to suit the requirements of commercial printing. It was partly for this reason that Hullmandel advocated such a painstaking method of cross-hatching to build up even tones and, conversely, that Engelmann's lavis lithographique and Tudot's manière noire were rejected.

Most of these topographical works were published in England and France. The catalogues of the J. R. Abbey collection of aquatinted and lithographed topography, 1770–186o,' list nearly 60o such publications with lithographed plates, whilc Adhémar's ‘Bibliographie des recueils de paysages lithographiés $\left( \mathrm { I } 8 \mathrm { I } 7 \mathrm { - } \mathrm { I } 8 \varsigma _ { 4 } \right) ^ { \flat _ { 2 } }$ contains 746 entries, of which 143 take the form of drawing lessons. It is hardly true to claim, as Adhémar does,³ that landscape lithography was almost unknown in England and seems to have been peculiar to France. It may well be that French publications outnumbered those produced in England, but even here we cannot be sure since the Abbey collection is not complete. The most ambitious work in scale and scope, Baron Taylor's Voyages pittoresques et romantiques dans l’ancienne France (Paris, 182o-78), was certainly published in France, but the most lavish works were produced in England. And while in France early lithographic draughtsmen spread their activities into other fields, such as caricature, military subjects, and portraiture, English lithographers tended to concentrate on landscape and topography and established such a reputation for it abroad that many of them were even invited to contribute to Taylor's publication. Hullmandel, while admitting the superiority of the French in figures and heads, considered that in landscape ‘every unprejudiced observer will say, that we can produce finer lithographic specimens than they'.⁴

Yet in neither of these two countries was topography held in very high esteem. Even landscape, which must in every respect be considered a loftier branch of painting than topography, occupied a very lowly position in the eyes of the Royal Academy and the juries of the Salons. It was not easily accommodated by a theory of aesthetics firmly established by the academies of the seventeenth century, and which by and large still prevailed, which required of a painting as a first necessity that the subject matter should be grand. Landscape became acceptable in the hands of Claude or Salvator Rosa only in as much as it embodied those very qualities which were more naturally to be found in history painting. Landscape was legitimate if it could conjure up the glories of a past civilization, as Claude's did, or, like Salvator's, express the heroic passions of man. But for a long time it was not considered a worthy subject in its own right, and until the middle of the nineteenth century occupied a lowly position at the bottom of the pictorial hierarchy

along with still life—the only other inportant branch of painting not concerned with the representation of man.

Eventually it was in the field of landscape that such ideas were challenged, first by Constable in England and later by the painters of the Barbizon in France. But their influence was not to be felt generally until after the middle of the nineteenth century, and in the period under consideration here the predominant feeling was still that expressed by Gainsborough on being invited to paint Lord Hardwicke's park:

Mr Gainsborough presents his humble respccts to Lord Hardwicke, and shall always think it an honor to be employ'd in anything for His Lordship; but with regard to real views from Nature in this country, he has never seen any place that affords a subject equal to the poorest imitations of Gasper or Claude.'

The whole concept of topography was too closely associated with utility to be worthy of the serious attention of painters; and when Fuseli, the Keeper of the Academy, referred to‘the last branch of uninteresting subjects, that kind of landscape which is entirely occupied with the tame delineation of a given spot'² he was merely giving the current definition of topography. But though not in accord with the lofty ideals of painting, topography found no such opposition in the field of print-making, which had for centuries accepted its status as a utilitarian craft with no great pretensions to ‘High Art' and had a long tradition of topography to its credit stemming from Reuwich's illustrations to Breydenbach, Sanctae Peregrinationes of 1486, through Hollar, Loggan, Carlevaris, Canaletto, Sandby, and Girtin to the nineteenth century.

1 Quoted by K. Clark, Landscape into art (Penguin Books, 1956), p. 48. 2 Ibid., p. 49.

# TOPOGRAPHY IN ENGLAND AND THE WATER-COLOUR TRADITION

VV HEN LITHOGRAPHY BEGAN TO EMERGE FROM ITS EXPERIMENTAL state in England towards the end of the second decade of the nineteenth century conditions were ripe for the growth of topographical lithography. Above all, there already existed a flourishing trade in topographical prints, and especially in sets of hand-coloured aquatint views. This process was ideally suited to the interpretation of drawings in the tinted style of watercolour then prevailing in England, and aquatint soon became the most natural way of reproducing them for publication. This relatively new process had been pioneered in England by Paul Sandby, and from its first use by him in a publication, Views in South Wales (1775–7), until the dawn of lithography, scores of such topographical works were published in England by the leading houses of the day.

An increased awareness of the beauty of British landscape was one of the main reasons for this interest in topography. There had been for some time a demand for representations of country seats of the nobility, such as the one Lord Hardwicke had invited Gainsborough to paint, but the writings of Rousseau had inspired a love of nature in the raw untampered by landscape architects. William Gilpin, Gilbert White, Thomas Bewick, Constable, and Wordsworth all reflect somcthing of this return to nature, and were responsible in their different ways for the appreciation of British scenery. It was particularly the more rugged parts that first attracted attention, and the Welsh mountains and the Lake District were favourite subjects for the illustrators of aquatint books. But by 182o these regions had been adequately covered, and lithographed topography in this country seems to show no special regional interest. All parts of the country were becoming increasingly accessible with the growth of an efficient system of transport, and particularly with the opening of the railways, and in this connection it is worth mentioning that the railways themselves provide a small but interesting category of lithographed topography in the thirties and forties.1

Topography was given an additional boost in 1815 by the capitulation of Napoleon's army and by the establishment of peaceful conditions on the continent for the first time in many years. There followed one of the great periods of travel to all parts of Europe, and the English traveller took with hin, as often as not, a pen to jot down his thoughts and a sketch-book to make a record of things seen. It was natural enough that some should make this their professional occupation, sometimes tidying up an amateur's drawing for publication and on other occasions making the original drawing, and numerous illustrated books of travel were produced to cater for those who wanted a reminder of their travels abroad. It is no accident that the sudden revival of lithography in 1818, after it had been dormant since the beginning of the century, should have coincided with this era of travel.

Though personal differences must have existed amongst such travellers, as a whole they showed no marked preference for any one country: France, Switzerland, Italy, Germany, the Netherlands, and later on for the more intrepid travellers, Spain and the Middle East, all attracted attention. The period was one of flux in taste, and we see at one and the same time a revival of medieval and classical studies, as well as a growing interest in what was then termed the Orient. The quest for relics of classical antiquity, such as that which led Lord Elgin to bring the Parthenon sculptures back to England, was inspired by the same romantic ideals that led to the study of medieval architecture in Normandy and the antiquities of ancient Egypt. The same sort of multiplicity of interest appears in the work of many of the most important artists of the tine: Blake, Fuseli, Flaxman, and Banks all used elements which derived from classical sources, but they can only be described as romantic in mood. Like many of the topographers of the period they turned to classical as well as medieval sources in the spirit of romanticism. It was this underlying romantic approach to history which reconciles the apparently contradictory and was responsible for this interest in Gothic Europe, classical antiquity, and the exotic East at more or less the same time. And it so happens that the period 1820-4o, which saw perhaps the greatest enthusiasm for Greek, Gothic, and Egyptian architecture, coincided with the rise of the topographical lithograph.

This period was also one of colonization and expansion for Britain. Exploration and travel to distant lands were largely the result of scientific curiosity; and scientific method, though not very advanced at the time, required that some representation be made of things seen, of the physical distribution of land, its vegetation and people, their customs and buildings. Before the days of photography it was virtually essential that an artist should accompany such expeditions to make a pictorial record to reinforce the printed word. Sometimes a professional would be employed especially to do this, as Westall was on Captain Flinders's ill-fated voyage to Australasia, but more often than not some skilful amateur member of the expedition would make a sketch which would then be made presentable for publication by a professional lithographer.i This type of illustrated travel book, carrying on the aquatint tradition, represents another important branch of topographical lithography.

Before considering some of the more important books and their lithographers it may be useful to give a brief account of the wider field of landscape painting in England from which the majority of topographers stemmed. The picture is by no means a clear one, but elements from three main traditions can be singled out for purposes of clarity. Still probably the prevailing influence was that of Claude who had established a type of ideal Italianate landscape which became the norm in eighteenth-century England. In his hands landscape was used to create a suitable setting for religious or mythological subjects, and trees and temples, on one or both sides of the picture, were used over and over again to frame the action and to open up a panoramic view bathed in the golden light of the Italian Campagna. This formula, saved by Claude's vision and handling from becoming stereotyped, was translated into English terms by men like Richard Wilson to lose much of its fascination. It had become the standard by which men judged landscape, whether real or pictorial. Nature herself, though a source book for

Claude, had become of secondary importance to many of his followers, and in time only those parts of nature which might have been found in a Claude landscape were thought fit for painting.

The second influence stemmed from Dutch landscape and particularly from Ruysdael. Unlike the ideal landscapes of Claude such paintings reveal an interest in the observation of the particular and the commonplace, and especially in those aspects of weather typical of north-west Europe— moving clouds and changing effects of light. The influence of this Dutch style of landscape, which spread from East Anglià by way of Crome and Constable, transformed the concept of the picturesque as established in the eighteenth century and created a new kind of picturesque landscàpe by the middle of the nineteenth century.

Thirdly, there existed a type of landscape painting which was more closely related to topography and which found its greatest exponent in Canaletto. Its particular characteristics were crisp, clear, sunlit views with large expanses of inactive sky, and a detailed delineation of the parts as well as the whole. Such paintings frequently seem to have been made from a viewpoint at eye level which gives a low horizon line, increases the speed of spatial movement into the picture, and at once puts the landscape on a thoroughly factual level by relating the spectator to the view represented. This type of landscape gained popularity in England with Canaletto's residence here from 1746 to 1753, and his influence can be traced in the work of Scott, Sandby, Girtin, and others.

Characteristics of each of these three approaches to landscape can be seen in English painting of the beginning of the nineteenth century. Only the last of them, with its interest in the recording of detailed information under ideal lighting conditions, can really be called topography. The probable use of the camera obscura by Canaletto, and certainly by many later topographers, helps to account for the lack of traditional pictorial composition in such work and explains its apparent relationship to photography. By contrast, ideal landscape and the whole concept of the picturesque is at variance with the needs of topography—the delineation of a particular locality whether beautiful or ugly. No ideal theory of art can accept topography unreservedly any more than it can accept accurate portraiture, but since both topographers and portrait painters have always considered themselves to be artists we find traditional ‘picturesque' elements creeping into their work. In topographical drawings this was particularly so in the foregrounds, where artists felt they had more freedom to extemporize, and it is in such parts that we find Claudian elements like the careful placing of a tree to frame a distant view. Such devices have become so much an accepted method of picture-making that we do not notice their repeated appearance even in the work of a painter like Constable, whose whole teaching rejects them. It is no easier to accommodate the Dutch tradition within the strict confines of topography, for it seeks to obscure with changing conditions of light and atmosphere the more permanent elements of form and detail; but concessions were made here as well to incorporate more genuine weather conditions into the essentially Mediterranean idiom of topography.

It is against this background of English landscape painting that we must consider the introduction of lithography to the realm of topographical illustration, which until then had been almost exclusively the province of aquatint and engraving. These two processes were ideally suited to topography proper with its insistence on crisp delineation and accurate detail of drawing; and this was particularly true of aquatint as it usually combined the linear process of etching with the actual tonal process of aquatinting, which tended to simplify tone and clarify form. The preliminary drawings for such prints were produced in water-colour. This medium owed its development to the print trade, and a type of painting was evolved with the prime aim of preparing drawings for reproduction. This resulted in the socalled tinted or stained style of water-colour painting which predominated in the eighteenth century and continued on well into the nineteenth, and might be placed somewhere half-way between drawing and painting. First of all the drawing was usually outlined in pen or pencil. This linear drawing was then given a monochrome foundation in wash and, finally, the local colour was applied on top of the monochrome basis. Such a break-down of procedure was very similar to that used in the production of an actual aquatint print, for the outline was etched first, the aquatint tone applied and usually printed in monochrome, and the whole then coloured in watercolour by a tean of assistants. The water-colours of Towne, Nattes, Sandby, and others would therefore have gone through much the same stages as the plates made from them and, consequently, it was not until almost the end of the eighteenth century that water-colour painting began to be regarded as an independent art form, and even then only grudgingly.

Lithography was invented just as this traditional type of topographical water-colour painting was being challenged by the younger generation of Girtin and Turner who, finding themselves freed from the necessity of working specifically for engraving, evolved a type of water-colour technique which relied less on outline drawing and more on tonal massing and atmospheric and light effects. At the same time the influence of the Romantic movement was beginning to have a liberating effect even in the field of topography. While aquatint was ideally suited to topography, the accurate recording of information about a given place, lithography was found to be better suited to romantic landscape, which required a more personal approach to the subject and a greater interest in those impermanent effects of nature that increase its emotive power. Strictly speaking these are qualities we should not expect to find in true topography which makes the impossible requirement that the artist's job is an impassive one, a mere recorder of things seen. The term is in fact much more easily defined than applied; but if aquatint is thought of as the process of topography then lithography, which allowed for more personal handling, must be considered more naturally the process of landscape.

All the same, after 182o lithography began to compete for the favours of artists in this very field of topography and soon attracted a number of them away from aquatint. The process of change was a gradual one. In the twenties lithographs, engravings, and aquatints are occasionally found together in one volume, and then usually to the detriment of lithography which could not compete in terms of precision with these other processes. In some publications it seems that the original intention was to have used lithography for all the plates, but that it proved unsatisfactory and was finally rejected. Aquatint was still in its heyday in the twenties, but by 183o lithography could hold its own and gradually absorbed the market the other had established during half a century. Lithographed topography reached its peak in terms of numbers in the forties and fifties, and in this period very few aquatint books were published at all. To a large extent the reason for this change to lithography must have been economic, but it does also reflect in the world of print-making and topography the larger movement in art and literature towards romantic ideals.

Despite these differences, English lithography inherited the aquatint tradition both in its style and in its close relationship with topography. With aquatint so well established in the early years of the nineteenth century it is not surprising that the first topographical lithographs should have been so strongly influenced by this process. The static character of Hullmandel's first lithographs, the struggle for a workable tonal process for lithography, as well as the use of terminology borrowed from aquatinting (Plates 8o, 81), all reflect this debt. But the strength of the native aquatint tradition was none the less a severe handicap to the rapid progress of lithography in England, and one of the reasons suggested by an early writer for England's lagging behind France in the first decades of lithography was that there was such a strong school of aquatint and engraving to compete with.

The most important legacy of the aquatint tradition to lithography was undoubtedly the close relationship between water-colour painting and print-making and, as far as the history of lithography is concerned, this was peculiar to England. In France it was primarily the oil painters who turned to the process, but in England nearly all the early lithographers were watercolour painters and, with a few exceptions, it would be difficult to name a lithographer of the first half of the nineteenth century who was not a watercolourist first and foremost. Because of this the whole development of lithography in England was determined by the requirements of watercolour painters, and not least by their concentration on topography.

Water-colours had nearly all been landscapes in the eighteenth century for reasons mentioned above, but the situation did not change very much when water-colour painting became more independent of engraving at the beginning of the nineteenth century. In 1804 and 1807 two societies were founded exclusively for the exhibition of water-colour paintings, which until then had been rather poorly hung at the Royal Academy. These were the Old Water-Colour Society and the New Society of Painters in Miniature and Water-Colours. A reviewer of their exhibitions in June 181o had this to say about their paintings in Ackermann's Repository of arts:

The first thing that strikes an observer, both at Spring-gardens and Bond-street, is, the overwhelming proportion of landscapes; a proportion almost as unreasonable as that of the portraits at Somerset-House. In pacing round the rooms, the spectator experiences sensations somewhat similar to those of an outside passenger on a mailcoach making a picturesque and picturizing journey to the north. Mountains and cataracts, rivers, lakes, and woods, deep romantic glens, and sublime sweeps of country, engage his eye in endless and ever-varying succession.i

Although water-colour painting was becoming freed from the dominance of the print trade the preoccupation with landscape and topography continued. In terms of scale water-colours could not compete with oils in the more important branch of history painting, but they had the advantage of portability which made them especially suited to landscape. But though water-colours were being exhibited and collected as such, many were still being produced specifically for print-making; and it was from the ranks of the water-colour societies that topographical lithographers were drawn. In this respect lithography was merely carrying on from, and continuing alongside, aquatinting. Of the founder members of the Old Water-Colour Society, Hills and Pyne had already contributed to the Specimens of polyautography, and Francis Nicholson and John Varley were to become important early lithographers. As lithography developed, many more of its members either drew on stone themselves or made drawings which were then translated by professional lithographic draughtsmen. Furthermore, Hullmandel, who did more than any other man to foster lithography in England, was a landscape water-colourist. So close was this connection between the two arts that when T. H. Fielding published the third edition of his treatise on painting in $\mathrm { I } 8 4 2 ^ { \mathrm { I } }$ he thought it necessary to add ‘an appendix, containing a Manual of Lithography', and he later composed a lithographic treatise himself.² It was almost inevitable therefore that there should have been a close similarity in style between water-colour painting and lithography; but the similarity was with the older tinted style of painting rather than with the more modern approach of Girtin, Constable, De Wint, and Cox. It is true that the older style had become less rigid under these newer influences, but it was not the innovators who turned to lithography. Girtin was, of course, already dead by the time lithography became firmly established in England, but Constable, Turner, and De Wint are not known to have produced any drawings on stone. Lithography was partly a commercial undertaking and unlikely therefore to be in tune with the most recent developments in painting and, in any case, it is very doubtful whether the process of lithography in its early stages could have accommodated itself to this new approach.

The water-colour painters who turned to lithography may be divided into two categories: those who made the original drawing and also put it on to stone, and those who mercly supplied a water-colour drawing to be copied by the lithographic draughtsman. These are by no mcans mutually exclusive categories as many artists worked in both capacities at different times, but we are most concerned here with those of the first group, for they were responsible for bringing to the technique of lithography many features of water-colour painting. They would have been encouraged to do so by their familiarity with the materials used for drawing on stone; crayon and pencil-brush presented no new problems, and the grain of the lithographic stone is very similar to that of a close-textured hand-made paper. The various attempts to imitate washes on stone from the dabbing style to Hullmandel's process oflithotint, in which liquid ink was applied to the stone with a brush, were at least in part the result of this need to imitate watercolour techniques on stone. The deep etching of white highlights on tint stones was likewise an attempt to imitate the appearance of touches of impasto white paint which some of the later water-colour painters liked to introduce when working'on tinted paper. The method of producing halftones from a solid tint stone by rubbing with a piece of cloth wrapped round the finger was a technique which stemmed directly from water-colour painting. Similarly, the use of the pencil-brush to indicate outlines and emphasize details, which became the normal method of bringing out foregrounds in the lithographs of Harding and Prout, was one of the technical peculiarities of the later topographers of the water-colour school. But perhaps the most remarkable similarity lies in the methods employed by both water-colourists and lithographers to obtain sharp and bright lights against darker grounds. Both paper and stone could be scratched away with a knife or scraper for effects such as rain and spray, or for highlights, but where rather larger light areas were needed lithographers adopted a technique very similar in principle to a method first communicated by the watercolourist Francis Nicholson to the Society of Arts in 1799, and later published in his treatise on water-colour painting. Nicholson's technique was to reserve the light areas of a drawing with a mixture of beeswax and turpentine, which was coloured with flake white and applied with a brush. When the water-colour was finished this composition could be dissolved by coaxing it away with a hog brush or rag soaked in turpentine. The process created considerable interest at the time, and since Nicholson was also a prolific lithographer it seems likely that the process of‘gumming out' light areas on the stone, which is described in the early treatises, owed its popularity partly to his influence. Nicholson used lithography to illustrate the second edition of his treatise, and a folding plate contains sketches printed by Hullmandel in the latter's dabbing style with the lights reserved in this way (Plates 82, 83).² Furthermore, Nicholson's process, though used to secure light areas rather than darks, is almost identical in principle to that described in Lithographic pencil drawing (1824), which appeared only a few years after the publication of his treatise.

Finally, English lithographed topography depended, as aquatint had done previously, on the addition of colouring by hand. Though such prints existed perfectly well in monochrome or with a tinted background, they were only consummated when coloured by hand. Usually the colours were transparent and were sparingly touched with varnish but, as in watercolour painting itself, opaque colours were used in later works. Not all topographical publications were hand-coloured, ànd not all copies of any one work, but a comparison of coloured and uncoloured versions clearly reveals the importance of the addition, and fully explains the difference in the original publication prices.' Extensive hand-colouring continued throughout the period of the topographical lithograph, and even where printed tints were used to support the black printing hand-colouring is still found in the more expensive copies. Since personal handling in lithography was generally freer and stronger than in aquatint the colouring was usually applied less in areas of flat tints, more with individual brush strokes. This reflects the changing style of water-colour painting in the nineteenth century from stained drawing, which was ideally suited to aquatint, to a more painterly approach better fitted to lithography. One thing that had not changed was the concept that a topographical print in its most expensive form should be an imitation water-colour, scarcely distinguishable from the original.

# ENGLISH TOPOGRAPHICAL LITHOGRAPHY 1818-1830

HE USE OF LITHOGRAPHY IN ENGLAND FOR THE ILLUSTRATION OF topographical works coincided with the general growth of the process after 1818, and can be more particularly related to improvements in the technical knowledge of English printers which made it possible for them to print satisfactorily from drawings made with chalk on stone. But one early example from the incunabula of lithography in the pen and ink style deserves mention. It is a collection of drawings by Thomas Barker entitled Thirty two lithographic impressions, from pen drawings of landscape scenery (Bath, 1814) (Plate 84).² The work is not strictly one of topography, but it ought to be considered in this context since it was the first important collection of landscape lithography to be published in England. Jagged crags, mountains, withered trees, bridges, and torrents abound in these sketches, which the artist felt would be ‘more valuable to the Lovers of the Picturesque' than his original plan of detached studies of trees and foregrounds. Thomas Barker (1769–1847), more usually known as Barker of Bath, was a successful painter of rustic scenes in the idiom of Gainsborough, and was best known in his lifetime for a popular painting of ‘The Woodman'. After a trip to Rome, 1791-3, he settled in Bath where these lithographs were produced. Barker had already contributed plates to both issues of the Specimens of polyautography and had also made further isolated prints in this period,¹ but he was one of the few artists of any significance who is known to have turned to lithography between this first experimenal period and the later development of the topographical school.

The first of Barker's important lithographed publications was a collection of Forty lithographic impressions of rustic figures (Bath, 1813). These are not so freely drawn as the landscape lithographs he produced in the following year and show rather more of the engravers' syntax of hatching.² Barker intimated in the advertisement to the Drawings of landscape scenery that he might produce a similar set of Italian landscapes, but this does not seem to have materialized. It has been suggested³ that Barker may also have been responsible for the landscape plates which served as illustrations to Bankes's treatise on lithography (Plate 11)4 as this was first published at Bath in the same year as the collection of Rustic figures. They certainly show much the same fluency in the handling of the pen as the Drawings of landscape scenery, and both accord with Bankes's advice to amateurs ‘that they do not on any account give their drawings any thing like the character of engraving . . .'.5 But if Barker was responsible for some of the plates it is hard to understand why Bankes made no mention of the fact, as he advertised Barker's forthcoming publications at some length in the text.6

Barker's Thirty two lithographic impressions, fromn pen drawings of landscape scenery was printed in the very small edition of fifty copies and was published complete. The size of the edition must have been linited more by an accurate assessment of the market than by technical factors, for Barker had found only 117 subscribers for his previous publication. At the time Bath possessed what was probably the only lithographic press in England which catered for the printing of artists' drawings, and it was there that the plates were Printed under the Direction of Mr. Barker, by D. Redman'.

The next lithographic press to start up in England after Redman's was probably the one opened by Ackermann in January 1817,7 which was mainly used for reproductive work in the Repository of arts and elsewhere. The greatest publisher of topographical books in this period found little use for lithography in a branch of work which was dominated by aquatint, and it must be admitted that the new process could not at that stage have competed with the older one in terms of clarity and precision. Only later, when lithography had proved itself, did Ackermann use it for topographical illustration, but by this time he had ceased to do his own printing and his contribution was that of publisher only. As a patron Ackermann did much to encourage lithography, as he did so many other branches of the polite arts, and it was he who published Senefelder's treatise and brought his invention to the notice of the Society of Arts in 1819.

All this encouragement was indeed necessary for the development of lithography, but what was needed more than anything else was a good lithographic printer. It was little use interesting artists and collectors in the process if drawings continued to be spoilt in the printing as they had been in the past. Redman had proved himself competent to print pen and ink drawings, but this was not the part of lithography which most attracted the artist. There already existed a number of graphic processes which could more or less reproduce a linear drawing. The real fascination of lithography, as far as the artist was concerned, lay in the possibility of multiplying an original chalk drawing made on stone with its infinite variety of tone. And this required a printer of far greater skill and wider interests than Redman— a man with some understanding of chemistry as well as the arts, and with the patience, tenacity, and also the means to apply knowledge gained through experiment.

Just such a man was Charles Joseph Hullmandel (1789–1850). He more than anyone else in England was responsible for the success of lithography, and particularly the topographical lithograph. Though of continental descent, with a French mother and German father, Hullmandel was born in Mayfair and apparently insisted on his English nationality. He was trained as a painter in Paris, and an examination of his sketches and published lithographs shows him to have been at his best a thoroughly competent landscape draughtsman. It was on the way back from a continental sketching tour in 1817 that Hullmandel first became interested in lithography and met Senefelder in Munich.² On his return to England he must have been anxious to try out the art for himself, and his first lithograph is preserved in his scrap-book in the

St. Bride Library (Plate 85).' It is inscribed ‘First drawing by me in 1818' and probably dates from the January of that year.² It is a rather coarse chalk drawing of a mountain scene, printed on buff paper and touched with Chinese white, and is not a particularly distinguished lithograph. Nevertheless, it shows Hullmandel as a draughtsman which, it must be stressed, was his original role in lithography. Only later did he turn to printing his own drawings and eventually those of other artists.

The change of roles came about over his dissatisfaction with the printing of his first published lithographs: Twenty-four views of Italy, drawn from nature, and engraved upon stone, by C. Hullmandel (London, 1818).3 This volume marks a turning point, not only in Hullmandel's career, but also in the history of English lithography; it is the first real topographical work to be produced by lithography in England, and also the first major venture in the use of chalk in this country.4 The book, a landscape medium folio, consists of twenty-four lithographs without text, and from a comparison of various states half the plates can be dated June 1818 and the other half 1o July of the same year. The single print mentioned above must in any case have been produced prior to the first of these two dates, and it is reasonable to assume therefore that, like the plates for the Twenty-four views of Italy, it was printed by Moser and Harris.

Very little is known about this firm of lithographic printers, the third of the three private presses known to have been working in England at that time.⁵ This is their first recorded printing, and from a circular preserved in the Victoria and Albert Museum6 it is known that the partnership was dissolved on I July 1818—that is, as Abbey has pointed out, half way through the production of Hullmandel's plates. Francis Moser is recorded printing again (on his own) drawings by Hullmandel and others in March 1819,1 and he also printed four lithographs for Harley's English scenery which were published in May 182o.² These, together with Hullmandel's drawings and the circular which was printed lithographically, are the only recorded examples of printing by Moser either with or without Harris.³

It was Hullmandel's dissatisfaction with the production of his Twentyfour views of Italy that persuaded him to turn to printing himself. He submitted these prints, together with others since lost, to the Society of Arts, and was awarded a silver fedal for them in 1819. The communication he submitted with the drawings was published in the Transactions of the society, and is dated 28 February 1819.

The drawings I have the honour to present to the Society for promoting the Arts, Manufactures, and Commerce, are the fruits of an art invented some years ago in Germany, and but lately introduced in this country, at least, in its present state of perfection. Its great advantage is that of enabling the artist to offer to the public the original productions of his pencil, without having recourse to engravers. The repeated failures I met with when I first began the work, intituled 'Twenty-four Views of Italy' determined me to have a press and materials of my own, and after several failures and renewed attempts during the space of fourteen months, I am at last enabled to offer some drawings which show, I hope, a decided progress in the art. I must beg leave to observe, that the twelve last of the 24 Views of Italy, and the 5 drawings marked 1, 2, 3, 4, and 5, are printed entirely under my direction, and that the preparation of the stones, the chalk, the ink, &c. are entirely done by myself. With regard to the drawings marked 6, 7, 8, 9, the whole process, from the first preparation of the stones, as well as the printing, are done also by myself.4

Unfortunately, the numbered drawings are no longer traceable, but the last four of these were probably Hullmandel's first attempt at printing his own lithographs; and a most ambitious one it must have been, for in the same communication he explains that he produced these ‘imitations of aquatinta'from several stones:⁵

The tints of the prints marked 6, 7, 8, 9, are produced by a second stone covered over with grease; the lights are scraped out in the places where they are intended to be; and the print being brought on the stone in its exact place, produces the effect of a drawing on coloured paper touched in with white. The drawings marked 7, 8, 9, are printed with a third stone, to give more effect to the fore ground.1

All the other prints mentioned must have been printed either by Moser and Harris or by Moser alone with Hullmandel directing operations on the spot (if this is what was meant by ‘entirely under my direction’) after Harris had left the firm.² But an examination of the prints shows no appreciable improvement in the printing done after Hullmandel's intervention, and we must assume that it was for this reason that he determined to experiment on his own account. He would have seen examples of German chalk work while in Munich, and perhaps it was the Oeuvres lithographiques, which contained tinted lithographs by Strixner, Piloty, and other artists, that prompted him to try this type of chalk lithography with tinted backgrounds, for nothing quite so ambitious had been produced in this manner in England before. Hullmandel would certainly have set much higher standards for the printing of chalk drawings than he could have expected from any English printer at the time, and must have realized that the only way to do justice to his own drawings was to print them himself.

But we must return to Hullmandel the lithographic draughtsman, for this he remained during his first few years of work as a printer, producing drawings for a number of publications. In this field too he was a pioneer in England, for up till this time all that was required of the artist was that he should draw on stone as he would on paper. It was Hullmandel who showed that lithography was something more than this, and he began to evolve a method of drawing with chalk on the stone which was to culminate in the complicated system of cross-hatching described in his treatise. His aim was undoubtedly to create a tonal system for lithography which could compete with aquatint in rendering an artist's watcr-colour. In his Twenty-four views of Italy the system was still very much in its infancy with rather coarse parallel hatchings borrowed from engraving (Plates 86–8). This style of working had been pioneered in Munich some ten years earlier by artists such as Joseph Hauber and Max Wagenbauer, whose lithographs in Lithographische Kunstprodukte (Munich, 1805-7) are quite as advanced in their use of systematic methods of chalk hatching as those of Hullmandel in Twenty-four views of Italy. Some of Hullmandel's early chalk lithographs are very similar both in technique and mood to the landscape lithographs of Wagenbauer, and it is quite possible that he had seen some of them while he was in Munich. In the plates of Twenty-four views of Italy the direction of shading usually follows the form; there is virtually no cross-hatching, and the lines appear aggressive in their regularity when compared with the carefully concealed techniques of later draughtsmen. But, for all the stiff monotony of his hatching, Hullmandel showed for the first time in this country that it was possible to draw in terms of tone with the lithographic crayon.

It would be difficult to over-emphasize Hullmandel's contribution to the development of lithography in England. As a draughtsman he was by no means outstanding, and yet his work at this crucial stage was probably vital to the subsequent growth of drawing on stone; as a theorist he was responsible for the translation of Raucourt's French treatise, besides writing one of his own which ran into several editions and other shorter notices as well; but it was as a printer of artists' lithographs, and particularly topographical drawings, that he had most influence on his contemporaries both in England and in France. From his introduction to topographical lithography until his death in 185o he was responsible for nearly all the major technical developments which affected this and many other branches of lithography. In terms of quantity he was responsible for printing plates for over 1 5o topographical publications alone. Amongst these may be counted many of the finest of the time, for he brought to the printing of artists' drawings the knowledge of an amateur scientist and the understanding and sympathetic handling of an artist, which inspired confidence in many of the leading water-colour painters of the day.

Hullmandel must have set up his lithographic press some time between 10 July 1818, when the latest plates of Twenty-four views of Italy were printed, and 28 February 1819 when he submitted his communication to the Society of Arts.¹ We must assume that he was not allowed to do his own printing at Francis Moser's and that his first real experience of this side of lithography was when he brought a press and materials from Germany and began experimenting with the help of his servant in his own home at 51 Great Marlborough Street. It was probably at this stage that he studied chemistry under Faraday in order to understand something of the chemical foundations of the process. In a very short time Hullmandel attracted a variety of amateurs and artists to his press and founded what amounted to almost a school of lithography at 51 Great Marlborough Street and, later on, at number 49 as well.

The first work drawn, printed, and published entirely by Hullmandel from this address was a set of three rather weak chalk drawings and a lithographed plan of the Château des Rochers (1819),' one of the few châteaux to escape the French Revolution unscathed. The drawings are all dated I May 1819 and are backed up with a yellowish tint stone with the lights reduced by scraping away in parallel hatchings in the way Hullmandel was later to recommend in his treatise. The technique is presumably much the same as the one he used for the drawings (nos. 6–9) which he sent to the Society of Arts and described in his communication. But this work is of little significance apart from its priority.

In the following year Hullmandel began publication of his Roman costumes; drawn from nature by Pinelli and C. Hullmandel (182o-1), a further set of lithographs similar in style, format, and subject to his Twenty-four views of Italy. Only three of the twenty-four plates are original compositions, the rest being very free adaptations of Pinelli's plates for Nuova raccolta di cinquanta costumi pittoreschi (Rome, 1816), but Hullmandel made all the drawings on the stone himself and was responsible for the printing of them as well. All the plates are in the style of chalk drawing he had evolved for his earlier Italian set, but they are drawn rather more freely and with a more complex, and at the same time less rigid, system of hatching. Hullmandel appears to have made no attempt to capture the spirit of the original etchings, and he converts Pinelli's austere and economical linear images into much more friendly naturalistic terms.

The publication that assured the success of lithography in England and, it was claimed, first drew the attention of artists to the process was G. B. Belzoni, Plates illustrative of the researches and operations in Egypt and Nubia (182o-2) (Plate 89).² The volume was one of a number which appeared in the 182os with plates produced by a variety of different processes, and along with the lithographs are found a number of etchings and one aquatint. Forty-four hand-coloured plates were published in 182o and the supplement of six plates (all lithographs) followed in 1822.¹ The twenty-six lithographed plates of the original volume were printed by ‘C. Hullmandel's lithography' from drawings by Belzoni himself and J. Ricci.² Belzoni had been attracted by the idea of having his drawings reproduced by lithography after seeing some sketches which Hullmandel had drawn and printed. No work of any importance had been printed in England by lithography at this stage and its use here, as one early writer pointed out,³ had the effect of recommending the new process to artists.4 With the establishment of Hullmandel's press a good printer of ckalk drawings was available in England for the first time, and from 182o onwards artists began to turn to the new process as well as to aquatint to have their drawings published. Nevertheless, aquatint was still flourishing in the 182os, and England had such a reputation abroad for this medium that her artists and engravers were even employed on a number of foreign publications.5 Against this cstablished tradition lithography could only hope to play second fiddle for some time to come.

One artist who was attracted to lithography at this early stage was the well-known water-colour painter Francis Nicholson (1753-1844).6He began working as a painter in oils, but became a frequent exhibitor with the Society of Painters in Water-Colour and was elected its president in 1813. Coming from Yorkshire, he specialized in a type of picturesque landscape readily found around him, and was particularly known for this kind of work. Though not amongst the greatest of the English water-colour school he was very successful in a commercial sense and also made important contributions to the technique of water-colour painting, broadening its scope and range of effects. In this respect, and particularly in his invention of a stopping-out varnish, he showed himself to have been something of an innovator; and it is in this light that we must view his introduction to lithography as he approached his seventieth year.

No established artist had yet turned to lithography in England, and certainly no one had let it interfere with the more important occupation of painting; but, according to the Redgraves, Nicholson virtually gave up the practice of his own art' and devoted ‘much time to the advancement of lithography'.i The earliest prints of his that can be traced were published in 182o and were printed either by Hullmandel or by Rowney and Forster, a firm whose first productions also date from that year.² These two firms were responsible for printing most of Nicholson's early drawings which were published, either plain or coloured, in paper wrappers. They are views of English scenery, particularly of his native Yorkshire, and at least four sets of Six views of Scarborough were published between 1822 and 1824.3 Nicholson made lithographs for no major publication but he must have been about the most prolific lithographer of the period; one contemporary writer estimates his output of drawings on stone as not much under 8oo,4 while Roget reports that he is credited with several hundred lithographs which, serving as “copies" for students, have been thumbed and torn and worn away like old school books, and consequently become rare'.s5 Like most successful water-colour painters Nicholson was also an instructor, and though this may not have been the prime reason for the production of these lithographs, it might well have accounted for their sale. He had long realized the value of producing more than one version of a drawing, especially for the Scarborough market where he found a ready sale for his work, and with an eye to business he had developed his own way of multiplying drawings:

For Scarborough, [he writes] I manufactured an incredible number of drawings. My process was by etching on a soft ground the different views of the place, from which werc taken impressions with black lead. This produced outlines so perfectly like those done by the pencil, that it was impossible to discover any difference.

This was nearly half of the work, and in the long days of summer I finished them at the rate of six daily.'

This passage relates to a period before Nicholson was forty years old, but it may help to explain why he was later attracted by lithography to the extent of giving up painting, and particularly why he produced so many views of Scarborough in this medium.

As far as is known Nicholson never left the British Isles and, apart from a few drawings after sketches done abroad by amateurs, his work is limited to views at home. This is true of his lithographs as well, which are mainly of scenery in Yorkshire, Wales, Scotland, and Devon. He was not an architectural draughtsman and preferred the wilder aspects of nature which he found in these parts; waterfalls and rocky scenes particularly interested him. In all this he was conforming to current ideas of the picturesque, and in lithography he frequently adopted the most picturesque of formats, the vignette.

Nicholson's technique of drawing on the stone (Plates 90, 91) was quite different from Hullmandel's. Whereas the latter usually built up his tones with chalk hatchings Nicholson often made use of Engelmann's lavis lithographique, the process that Hullmandel adopted and called the dabbing style. This method was ideally suited to a water-colour painter who was used to building up his tones stage by stage, and it was especially suited to Nicholson because the method of reserving lights which Engelmann advocated was very similar to the process he had been using in his water-colours to preserve the light areas from dark washes.² The dabbing style was a much surer way of obtaining tonal unity than Hullmandel's chalk shading as seen in its early stages in Twenty-four views of Italy. It was also a more successful method for producing softer greys in skies and distances, but it lacked the crispness of good chalk work. Nicholson often employed the two together, combining the advantages of both methods, but he rarely employed a rigid shading technique, preferring the chalk to caress the stone as if it were paper. Whereas Hullmandel's method of drawing tended towards severity Nicholson's appeared at its best, soft and delicate, at its worst, woolly and indecisive. A generation older than other early lithographers, Nicholson stands somewhat apart from them; he does not appear to have had any great influence on his contemporaries and continued to produce his own sets of lithographed views without ever contributing to any of the major publications in this medium.

One such work, the most important containing topographical lithographs published in England in the 182os, was Britannia delineata: comprising views of the antiquities, remarkable buildings, and picturesque scenery of Great Britain. Kent (London, 1822–3) (Plates 92, 93). It is an imperial folio volume with a dedication to George IV and notes to the plates in English and French. The original plan, in obvious emulation of the Voyages pittoresques et romantiques dans l'ancienne France, which began publication'two years earlier, was to cover the whole of Great Britain county by county; but only twenty-five plates for this first volume on Kent were ever published.' These appeared in five parts of five plates each with dates ranging from February 1822 to September 1823. Even this first volume does not seem to have been completed, for Rodwell and Martin announced in one of their publication lists (undated) that the volume on Kent was to be completed in eight numbers, each containing five plates. This figure is confirmed by a publisher's note found with part four of Britannia delineata² and dated January 1823, which claimed that the first volume will be completed in four additional Numbers, forming eight in all, and will certainly appear in the course of the present Year'. No explanation can be found for this halt in the progress of a venture which had all the ingredients of success, but there must have been some delay in the appearance of the parts even before publication ceased. It is not even clear who in fact published the work, for the plates of the first four parts carry the imprint of Rodwell and Martin (who certainly announced the first three parts in their lists), while those of part five bear that of Ackermann. The subject is even more confused as a set of five parts in their original wrappers in the John Johnson Collection is described by pasted correction slips on the wrappers themselves as having been published by J. Dickinson, 114 New Bond Street, while the original imprint found beneath them is Printed for R. Ackermann, Strand'.3

The significance of Britannia delineata for the historian of lithography is that it brought together for the first time three of the leading lithographic draughtsmen of the period. Hullmandel, who printed all the plates, was responsible for eleven drawings in an improved but still rather laboured style of hatching (Plates 94–6), Harding for eight (Plates 107–12), and Prout and Westall three each (Plates 98-101, 105). For none of them was it the first excursion into lithography, but the publication provides a good opportunity to compare styles and to discuss their lithographic work.

Samuel Prout (1783-1852) was the first of these to experiment with lithography. In fact, he was working on stone before either Hullmandel or Nicholson, for he contributed a chalk seascape to Ackermann's Repository of arts as early as April 1817.' Like most lithographers of the topographical school Prout was primarily a water-colour painter. He was born in Plymouth and specialized in views of the west country and particularly coast scenes until, after a visit to the continent in 1818, he turned increasingly to the drawing of foreign cities. For the rest of his life Prout devoted himself to making architectural drawings of French, Belgian, Swiss, and Italian towns, many of which became popular by being published in the form of lithographs.
